The Mislabeling of Sects: Jabariyya, Qadariyya, and Mu'tazila
Maturidi clarifies the misnaming of sects and argues that the Mu'tazila are actually closer to jabr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the labels 'Jabariyya' and 'Qadariyya' are misapplied by different sects, and how the Mu'tazila's own position is closer to jabr.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila accuse Ahl al-Sunna of jabr, but they themselves hold a view that implies compulsion.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi addresses a historical controversy over names. The Mu'tazila label Ahl al-Sunna as 'Jabariyya' because they deny power before the act, but Maturidi turns the tables: the Mu'tazila's own view—that power exists only at the moment of action—leads to jabr, since action occurs at a time when there is no effective power. This lesson clarifies the proper understanding of power and agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sects mentioned
Read the passage and note the three groups: Jabariyya, Qadariyya, and Mu'tazila. Understand how they label each other.
Maturidi mentions that the Mu'tazila call Ahl al-Sunna 'Jabariyya'.
2
Understand the counter-argument
Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila's own position on power—that it exists only at the moment of action—is actually closer to jabr because action occurs without effective power.
This is a rhetorical reversal.
3
Examine the analogy with senses
Maturidi uses the analogy of sight and hearing to support his point: perception fails when the sense is absent, just as action is impossible without power.
This strengthens the argument that power must be present as a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد احتجوا بما في القرآن من ذكر الاستطاعة، وقد بينا ذلك الوجه، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم الجبرية المعروفة عندنا هم الذين يلقبوا بالجبر، وأحالوا القدرة على ما في فعل جعل الله كاذبا، وأرجعوا جميع الأفعال إلى الله، ولم يثبتوا للعباد في التحقيق فعلا. قيل: يقول لهم الله: لم فعلتم ذا، ولم لا فعلتم ذا؟ ونقول: افعلوا ذا، ولا تفعلوا ذا في التحقيق، بل إن أمر أو نهى فإنما يأمر في التحقيق نفسه وينهى نفسه، ثم هو يرتكب المنهي في التحقيق، ويأمر ويطيع هو في الحقيقة، يعاقب غيره فيعذبه ويثيبه، ويسميه مع هذا حكيما رحيما جل من صفته الرحمة والحكمة، وعلى ذلك يجب أن لا يجدوا الألم في الحقيقة واللذة، وتكون حقيقتها راجعة إلى الله جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى. ثم يبطل معنى الرسل والكتب في التحصيل تصير إلى الله بالأمر والنهي والوعد والوعيد، لا منه إلى غيره ثم يبطل حكمة خلق الخلق ويحصل على العبث إن كان العلم يبلغ معرفته، ومن يكون خروج فعله على كفران وجحود المنن، والكذب في الإخبار، والسفه في الأفعال فهو حقيق أن يكون شيطانا رجيما، فهو كذلك لا ريب فيه، وهو ما شبيه بقولهم: كان الله غير عالم ولا قادر، ثم صار كذلك، فلعل تدبيره الأفعال التي كانت فما نسب إلى الخلق في ذلك الوقت جل الله وتعالى عن ذلك وهم الذين يلقبوا بالاعتزال - الخبر إلينا، على تبرينا كما ثم نسب القدرية عن ذلك عقدا وقولا، لكن كتبهم في هذا نحو كذبهم علينا في اسم القدرية ثم تذكر أحقنا ذلك في مقابلة المذهبين؛ ليعلموا جرأة المعتزلة وعظيم سفههم بينا في القدرية - وادعوا علينا اسم الجبر بانكارنا كون قدرة الفعل قبل وقته، ثم هم حققوا الفعل في وقت لا قدرة فيه، وتحقيق الفعل في وقت الوصف بلا قدرة أقرب إلى معنى الجبر من تحقيقها مع الفعل لمن عقل الجبر والاختيار. ومما يوضح ذلك أن الفعل غير متوهم في حال العجز، ومتوهم وجوده في حال ارتفاع العجز، فكان توهمه مع الارتفاع أرفع وأبلغ من توهمه مع الوجود؛ إذ هو سبب، فكذلك القدرة التي هي سبب الفعل في الحقيقة، ويؤيد ذلك فساد الدرك مع ذهابه بما تقدم من البصر، وكذا السمع وعمل كل الحواس، فلذلك المنع بالبصر
And they also argued by what is in the Quran of mention of capability (استطاعة), and we have already explained that aspect. And there is no power except with Allah. Then the Jabariyya known among us are those who are labeled with jabr (compulsion), and they attributed power to that [i.e., they denied power to the servant] and made God a liar in [His] action, and they referred all actions to God, and they did not affirm for the servants any effective act. It is said: God would say to them: Why did you do this? And why did you not do this? And we say: Do this, and do not do this, in reality—but rather, if He commands or forbids, He only commands in reality Himself and forbids Himself, then He commits the forbidden in reality, and He commands and obeys in truth, and He punishes others and torments them and rewards them, and He calls Himself wise and merciful—glory be to Him whose attributes are mercy and wisdom. According to that, they should not feel pain in reality nor pleasure, and their reality would revert to God—glory be to Allah above that and exalted. Then the meaning of messengers and books would be nullified in effect, turning to God in command, prohibition, promise, and threat—not from Him to others. Then the wisdom of creating creation would be nullified, and it would result in futility, if knowledge could attain His knowledge. And whoever's action results in ingratitude and denial of favors, and lying in reports, and foolishness in actions, he is worthy to be an accursed devil. He is thus without doubt. And this resembles their saying: Allah was not knowing nor powerful, then He became so. Perhaps His management of actions that were and then were attributed to creation at that time—glory be to Allah and exalted above that. And they are the ones who are labeled as Mu'tazila—the report reaching us concerning our exoneration—as also. Then the Qadariyya labeled us concerning that as belief and saying, but their books in this are like their lying about us in the name Qadariyya. Then we mentioned our proof (?) in opposition to the two schools, so that they may know the audacity of the Mu'tazila and the greatness of their foolishness among the Qadariyya—and they claimed against us the name of Jabriyya because of our denial that power of action exists before its time, but they realized the action at a time when there is no power. And realizing the action at the time of description without power is closer to the meaning of jabr than realizing it with action, for one who understands jabr and choice. And what clarifies that is that action is not conceivable in a state of incapacity, while its existence is conceivable in a state of removal of incapacity, so conceiving it with removal is higher and more profound than conceiving it with existence, because it is a cause. Likewise, power which is the cause of action in reality, and that is supported by the corruption of perception with its removal as preceded with sight, and likewise hearing and the operation of all senses. Therefore, the prevention is with sight.
Source pages p. 918

Commentary
This passage refutes the Jabariyya (those who affirm absolute compulsion) and clarifies the mislabeling of sects. Maturidi begins by noting that the Qadariyya also argued from Quranic mentions of capability, but this is already addressed. Then he describes the Jabariyya as those who deny any effective action to servants, attributing all acts to God, which leads to contradictions: if God commands or forbids but is the sole agent, then He commands Himself, forbids Himself, commits the forbidden, and punishes others—making God inconsistent with wisdom and mercy. This nullifies the purpose of messengers, books, and the wisdom of creation, leading to absurdity. Such a view is comparable to saying God was not knowing or powerful then became so. Then Maturidi discusses the naming: the Mu'tazila claim that Ahl al-Sunna are Jabariyya because they deny power before the act, but in fact the Mu'tazila themselves realize the action at a time when there is no power (since they claim power precedes but does not last), which is closer to jabr. Moreover, action is inconceivable during incapacity, but conceivable when incapacity is removed—so power as a cause is valid. This is analogous to how senses function. The passage ends with a note about perception with sight.

Key Terms
جبرية
قدرة
اعتزال
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahl al-Sunna are Jabariyya because they deny power before action.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazila's own view leads to jabr, and the correct position affirms power as a cause concurrent with action.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is ironic about the Mu'tazila's accusation of jabr?
A) They themselves hold a view closer to jabr
B) They deny power entirely
C) They affirm power before action
D) They agree with the Jabariyya

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's own theory of power makes them more deserving of the label 'Jabariyya' than Ahl al-Sunna.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have accused someone of a fault in belief or practice, have you truly examined whether you might be guilty of something similar? What would it mean for you to admit that possibility and seek correction?
?
The Mu'tazila were quick to label others as 'Jabariyya' without fully understanding their own position. In your own life, where are you quick to label or judge others without first checking your own heart? Name one specific relationship where you need to replace accusation with humility.
?
This lesson shows that even theological debates can be driven by misrepresentation. Is there a person or group you have dismissed based on a secondhand description rather than their own words? Take one step this week to learn their actual position directly.

The Absurd Consequences of Denying Human Capacity
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazila by showing logical consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazili position on divine command by exposing its absurd logical consequences.

Core Idea
Denying human capacity forces God to command Himself and nullifies prophethood.

Imam Maturidi engages with the Mu'tazila who claimed that God commands and forbids humans. He shows that if humans have no real capacity, then God's commands become self-addressed, stripping prophetic missions of their meaning and rendering creation futile.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the English translation carefully.
2
Identify the first absurdity
Notice how Maturidi argues that if humans have no real capacity, then God's commands and prohibitions are actually directed to Himself, and He would then commit what He forbids.
This is the core logical consequence of the Mu'tazili denial of human power.
3
Trace the nullification of messengers
See how this leads to the nullification of the meaning of messengers and books, as they become a dialogue between God and Himself rather than guidance for creation.
Also nullifies the wisdom of creating creation, leading to futility.
4
Consider the comparison
Maturidi compares their view to saying God was not knowing or powerful and then became so, which is an absurd attribution of change to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلا . قيل : يقول لهم الله : ليم فعلتم ذا ، وليم لا فعلتم ذا ؟ ونقول : إفعلوا ذا ، ولا تفعلوا ذا في التحقيق، بل إن أمر أو نهى فإنما يأمر فى التحقيق نفسه وينهى نفسه ، ثم هو يرتكب المنهى فى التحقيق، ويأمر ويطيع هو في الحقيقة ، يعاقب غيره فيعذبه ويثيبه ، ويسميه مع هذا حكيما رحيما جل من صفته الرحمة والحكمة ، وعلى ذلك يجب أن لا يجدوا الألم في الحقيقة واللذة ، وتكون حقيقتها راجعة إلى الله جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى . ثم يبطل معنى الرسل والكتب فى التحصيل تصير إلى الله بالأمر والنهى والوعد والوعيد ، لا منه إلى غيره ثم يبطل حكمة خلق الخلق ويحصل على العبث إن كان العلم يبلغ معرفته ، ومن يكون خروج فعله على كفران وجحود المنن ، والكذب فى الإخبار ، والسفه في الأفعال فهو حقيق أن يكون شيطانا رجيما ، فهو كذلك لا ريب فيه ، وهو شبيه بقولهم : كان الله غير عالم ولا قادر ، ثم صار كذلك ، فلعل تدبيره الأفعال التي كانت فما نسب إلى الخلق في ذلك الوقت جل الله وتعالى عن ذلك
In reality, it is said: God says to them: Why did you do this, and why did you not do this? And we say: Do this, and do not do this in reality, but if He commands or forbids, He only commands in reality Himself and forbids Himself. Then He commits the forbidden in reality, and He commands and obeys in truth. He punishes others, tormenting them, and rewards them, and calls Himself with this wise and merciful—exalted is He who has mercy and wisdom as attributes. And upon that, they should not feel pain in reality nor pleasure, and their reality returns to God—exalted and high is God above that. Then the meaning of messengers and books in attainment is nullified; they become to God by command, prohibition, promise, and threat, not from Him to others. Then the wisdom of creating creation is nullified, and futility is obtained if knowledge reaches His knowledge. And whose action comes out as ingratitude, denial of favors, lying in reports, and foolishness in actions, he is deserving to be a cursed devil. So he is like that, no doubt. And it is similar to their statement: God was not knowing nor powerful, then He became so. Perhaps His management of the actions that were then attributed to creation at that time—exalted and high is God above that.
Source pages p. 919

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position by exposing its logical consequences. Maturidi begins by presenting the Mu'tazili claim: God asks why people do or don't do something, and then He commands or forbids. But Maturidi argues that according to their view, when God commands or forbids, He is actually commanding and forbidding Himself in reality, because they deny that human actions have any real capacity independent of God. Then He (God) commits what He forbids, and He commands and obeys Himself, yet He punishes others for it while calling Himself wise and merciful. This destroys the meaning of messengers and books, as they become a dialogue between God and Himself rather than guidance for creation. It also nullifies the wisdom of creation, making it futile. Anyone whose actions are characterized by ingratitude, lying, and foolishness is worthy of being a cursed devil. Maturidi then compares this to the absurdity of saying God was not knowing or powerful and then became so, implying that their view attributes change to God. He ends by declaring God exalted above such notions.

الأمر
النهي
الحكمة
العبث
الشيطان الرجيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila actually claim that God commands His own essence.
Correction: No, Maturidi argues that their denial of human capacity logically forces that conclusion; they do not explicitly hold it.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is nullified by the Mu'tazili view of divine command?
A) The oneness of God
B) The meaning of messengers and books
C) The existence of evil
D) The power of God

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazila by exposing that their denial of human capacity leads to absurdities like God commanding Himself, nullifying prophethood, and attributing change to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does Maturidi’s argument expose that such thinking turns God into the one who both commands and disobeys? What one sin will you stop excusing today and take full responsibility for, knowing that your capacity and choice are real?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were defending God’s honor, but their denial of human capacity led them to attribute change and injustice to Him. Is there any area of your understanding of Allah where you have tried to defend His majesty using your own logic rather than the revealed truth? Will you submit to the divine balance of God creating deeds and humans acquiring them, even if your mind struggles? What practical step will you take to align your belief with the Quran and Sunnah on this issue?

The Mu'tazila's Charge of Jabr Against the Husayniyya
Maturidi discusses the dispute between Mu'tazila and Husayniyya over the naming of jabr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basis of the Mu'tazila's accusation that the Husayniyya are jabriyya, and how Maturidi frames the dispute as one of terminology rather than substance.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's charge of jabr hinges on a narrow definition of human power.

This lesson examines a sectarian dispute over the label 'jabriyya'. The Mu'tazila accused the Husayniyya of denying human power over opposite actions, but Maturidi points out the disagreement is largely semantic. By reading this passage, you'll see how the definition of power (qudra) shapes theological accusations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the key terms: الحسينية, المعتزلة, الجبر, القدرة, اللطف.
The Arabic text is incomplete; the passage ends abruptly.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's charge
The Mu'tazila call the Husayniyya 'jabriyya' because they restrict human power to only the action being performed, not its opposite. Understand why this leads to the label of compulsion.
3
Analyze the Husayniyya's position
The Husayniyya affirm that the servant has power over the action he is doing and choice over it. They also claim that God withholds some grace (lutf). Compare this with the Mu'tazila's claim that God gives everything beneficial.
4
Understand Maturidi's framing
Maturidi states that the real difference is only in the name. Both groups agree on the amount of power given, but differ on whether the servant has power at the time of action. Reflect on how this shifts the debate from substance to terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سمت المعتزلة الحسينية مجبرة بما قالت الحسينية للعبد قدرة ما هو فيه من الفعل، وليست له قدرة ضده وقت الفعل وقبل ذلك الوقت: الاختلاف بيننا وبين المعتزلة إنما هو في الاسم ما هو فيه خاصة؛ لأن الحسينية تقول هو على فعند الله لطف لم يعطه، والمعتزلة يقولون: لم يبق عند الله شيء فيه صلاحه إلا وقد أعطى، فقد اتفقا على قدر ما أعطاه، ولا قوة له وقت الفعل عند المعتزلة، وعند الحسينية له قدرة ما هو فيه وله اختيار ما هو فيه، فكان الذي معه من
Then the Mu'tazila called the Husayniyya 'jabriyya' because the Husayniyya said that the servant has a power only over what he is doing of the action, and he does not have power over its opposite at the time of the action and before that time. The difference between us and the Mu'tazila is only in the name specifically; because the Husayniyya say he is upon... for God there is a grace He did not give, and the Mu'tazila say: nothing remains with God of what is for his benefit except that He has given it. So they agreed on the amount He gave, and there is no power for him at the time of action according to the Mu'tazila, while according to the Husayniyya he has power over what he is doing and he has choice over it, so what he has with him is...
Source pages p. 920

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the dispute between the Mu'tazila and the Husayniyya (followers of Husayn al-Najjar) regarding the naming of 'jabr' (compulsion). The Mu'tazila label the Husayniyya as 'jabriyya' because the Husayniyya hold that the servant has power only over the action he is currently performing, not over its opposite, either at the time of action or before. Maturidi then points out that the real disagreement is merely about terminology. The Husayniyya say that God has withheld some grace (lutf) from the servant, while the Mu'tazila claim that God has given everything beneficial. Both agree on the amount of power given; according to the Mu'tazila, the servant has no power at the time of action, while the Husayniyya affirm that he has power and choice over what he is doing. The text ends abruptly, likely continuing the argument.

Key Terms

المعتزلة
الجبر
القدرة
اللطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Husayniyya are full Jabriyya who deny all human power.
Correction: The Husayniyya actually affirm that the servant has power over his current action and choice over it, but not over its opposite. Thus they are not completely deterministic.

Checkpoint
Why did the Mu'tazila call the Husayniyya 'jabriyya'?
A) Because Husayniyya deny all human power
B) Because Husayniyya say the servant has power only over his current action, not its opposite
C) Because Husayniyya claim equality with God
D) Because Husayniyya reject divine grace

Key Takeaway
The charge of jabr between Mu'tazila and Husayniyya reduces to a disagreement about the scope of human power at the time of action, not a denial of all human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi observes that the Mu'tazila and Husayniyya agreed on the amount of power God gives, yet they called each other names. Is there a person or group you label negatively even though you might actually agree on the core facts? What label do you need to drop today to see that you agree more than you think?
?
The Husayniyya affirm 'power over what he is doing and choice over it'. They are not denying agency. Are there areas of your life where you claim to believe in God's predestination but secretly blame Him for your choices, effectively acting as a jabri? How can you honestly affirm both God's sovereignty and your responsibility right now?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted that God gives every beneficial grace, while the Husayniyya said He withholds some. When you face hardship, do you assume God has given you everything you need, or do you accuse Him of holding back? What does your reaction reveal about your belief in divine justice?

Refuting Khawarij Using Prophets' Minor Sins
Maturidi refutes Khawarij who declare minor sins as disbelief by citing prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the Khawarij claim that minor sins constitute disbelief by citing the example of prophets and awliya.

Core Idea
Minor sins do not negate prophethood or faith.

The Khawarij held that committing minor sins removes a person from faith. Maturidi counters this by pointing to the prophets and awliya, who themselves were afflicted with minor sins yet remained prophets and saints. This passage demonstrates the logical and theological flaw in the Khawarij's extreme position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage first, then the English translation. Notice the key terms: صغائر (minor sins), كفر (disbelief), أنبياء (prohets).
2
Identify the Khawarij claim and Maturidi's refutation
The Khawarij claim that minor sins lead to kufr. Maturidi responds by noting that prophets were afflicted with minor sins, yet their prophethood continued. Therefore, minor sins cannot be kufr.
3
Reflect on the consequence of their extremism
Maturidi argues that the Khawarij, in their exaggeration, end up declaring disbelief in the prophets, which is itself the greatest sin. This shows their transgression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي ينقض قول الخوارج الذين يكفرون بالصغائر ما بلى بها الأنبياء والأولياء ، وما يكفر يسقط النبوة والولاية ، ومن ذلك وصف إيمانه بالانبياء فهو كافر بهم ، فبلغ من تعظيمهم الذنوب إلى أن كفروا به ، وهو أعظم الذنوب، وهذا حق من تعدى حدود الله فى الحكم وغلا في دين الله أن يكون عطبه أرجى ما يكون عنده من أسباب النجاة ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then that which refutes the saying of the Khawarij who declare disbelief due to minor sins: what the prophets and awliya were afflicted with, and what disbelief does not remove prophethood and sainthood. And from that is describing his faith with the prophets, so he is a disbeliever in them. So they reached in their magnification of sins to the point that they disbelieved in Him, and that is the greatest of sins. And this is true of one who transgresses the bounds of God in judgment and exceeds in the religion of God; that his destruction is more hope than what he has of causes of salvation. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 927

Commentary
This passage refutes the Khawarij position by noting that prophets themselves were afflicted with minor sins (zallat) yet their prophethood was not nullified. The Khawarij, in exaggerating the gravity of minor sins, end up declaring disbelief in the prophets, which is itself the greatest sin. This shows their transgression and extremism.

Key Terms
صغائر
كفر
أنبياء
أولياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is defending the sinlessness of prophets.
Correction: Maturidi actually argues that prophets committed minor sins but that does not negate their prophethood.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi use the example of prophets to refute the Khawarij?
A) Prophets are sinless
B) Prophets committed minor sins but remained prophets
C) Prophets never committed sins
D) Prophets only commit major sins

Key Takeaway
Minor sins do not constitute disbelief, as evidenced by the prophets who committed them without losing prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself magnifying the faults of others while overlooking your own? How does the example of the prophets' minor sins challenge you to be more merciful and balanced in your judgments?
?
Have you ever been so strict in your religious practice that you unwittingly rejected or condemned someone whom Allah loves? What steps can you take to guard against the Khawarij mentality within yourself?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim commit a sin, is your first instinct to correct with harshness or to remember that your own salvation is not guaranteed? How can the warning in this passage about transgressing God's bounds lead you to humble yourself and seek forgiveness?

Refuting the Mu'tazila Using Prophets' Fear
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazila using prophets' supplication for forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses the supplication and fear of prophets to refute the Mu'tazila claim that minor sins are always forgiven and punishment is not in wisdom.

Core Idea
Prophets' fear of punishment proves minor sins can be punishable.

The Mu'tazila argue that Allah forgives minor sins and that punishing them is not in wisdom. Imam Maturidi counters by pointing to the Qur'anic description of prophets who supplicate with fear and humility over their slips, begging for forgiveness. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows how it refutes both the Mu'tazila and the Khawarij.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's argument
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. This is a dense theological argument with a clear logical structure.
2
Recall the Mu'tazila position
Remember that Mu'tazila believe Allah automatically forgives minor sins and that it would be unjust to punish them. This is the view Maturidi is refuting.
Mu'tazila also deny that Allah can punish anyone who dies with faith, even for minor sins.
3
Examine the prophetic evidence
Notice how Allah describes prophets as supplicating in fear and humility, weeping over their slips (zalat) and being answered. This implies their slips were potentially punishable.
Prophets are infallible but commit minor slips.
4
Follow the logical inference
If the prophets feared punishment, then their slips must be such that punishment could be expected. This directly contradicts the Mu'tazila claim that punishment for minor sins is not in wisdom.
Maturidi uses a hypothetical: if punishment were not possible, the prophets' fear would be misplaced.
5
Extend to the Khawarij refutation
The same evidence also refutes the Khawarij, who say sinners (minor or major) are no longer believers. If prophets' slips caused disbelief, they would not remain prophets.
The passage clearly refutes both sects in one conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى قول المعتزلة فى ذلك وصف الله الأنبياء بالدعاء له تضرعا وخفية وطمعا وخوفا وببكائهم على ما كان منهم من الزلات وتضرعهم إليه حتى أجيبوا في دعائهم وأعطوا سؤلهم ، ولو لم تكن ذنوبهم بحيث احتمال التعذيب عليها في الحكمة ، أو كان عليهم من ذلك خوف التعذيب لكان فى ذلك تعدى الحد والوصف بالجود [ ] والتعدى منه وذلك أعظم من الزلات . فهذا ينفى قول المعتزلة في اثبات المغفرة فى الصغائر وإخراج فعل التعذيب عن الحكمة ، وقول الخوارج بازالة اسم الإيمان ، عنه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And upon the saying of the Mu'tazila in that: God described the prophets as supplicating to Him in humility and secrecy and hope and fear, and with their weeping over what occurred from them of slips, and their humbling themselves to Him until they were answered in their supplications and given their requests. And if their sins were not such that bearing punishment upon them is in wisdom, or if they had fear of punishment from that, then in that there would be transgression of the limit and description with generosity [], and transgression from it is greater than the slips. So this refutes the saying of the Mu'tazila regarding establishing forgiveness for minor sins and removing the act of punishment from wisdom, and the saying of the Khawarij removing the name of faith from him. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 927

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila by noting that God described prophets as supplicating in fear and humility over their slips, which implies that their slips were such that punishment could be expected. If the Mu'tazila claim that minor sins are always forgiven and punishment is not in wisdom, then the prophets' fear would be pointless. Thus, the Mu'tazila position is invalid. Similarly, the Khawarij are refuted because if prophets' slips were kufr, they would not remain prophets.

Correction: Maturidi shows that prophets feared punishment for their minor slips, implying that such punishment is possible within divine wisdom.

Key Takeaway
Prophets' fear and supplication for forgiveness demonstrate that their minor slips were potentially punishable, refuting the Mu'tazila doctrine of automatic forgiveness and the Khawarij doctrine of removing faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recall a minor mistake you made today, did you feel a sense of fear and humble yourself before Allah, or did you dismiss it as too small to matter? How much do you truly believe that every slip, however small, is recorded and could be punished if not repented?
?
The prophets, despite their high station, wept over their slips. When was the last time you actually wept or felt real remorse over a sin? If you haven't, what does that say about your awareness of Allah's justice and your own accountability?
?
If you knew that a single minor sin could be punished unless you repent, would you change the way you approach your daily actions? What is one minor habit you constantly excuse that you need to treat as a serious offense starting today?

Positions on Major Sinners Among Muslims
Maturidi enumerates six Muslim positions on major sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six distinct theological positions held by early Muslim scholars regarding the status of Muslims who commit major sins, as enumerated by Imam al-Maturidi.

Core Idea
Major sins divided the early Muslim community into six distinct theological positions.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi presents an objective survey of the various views held by early Muslim scholars and sects on the fate of those who commit major sins. He lists six distinct positions, ranging from considering the sinner a disbeliever to suspending judgment on the divine threat. This lesson will guide you through each of these views and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own enumeration come through before interpreting.
Notice that Maturidi begins by stating that major sins are subject to forgiveness, then lists the positions.
2
Identify each position
Maturidi lists several views: kafir, mushrik, neither believer nor disbeliever, munafiq, believer but fasiq, and waqf (suspension). Count them and note the reasoning behind each.
The last position (waqf) involves suspending judgment on God's threat.
3
Understand the distinction between minor and major sins
Maturidi then relates the positions to the difference between minor and major sins, arguing that forgiveness is possible for minor sins, so the threat must apply to major sins.
4
Reflect on the Quranic evidence
He cites verses about despairing of God's mercy to support the view that denying forgiveness leads to disbelief. Consider how this connects to the positions.
This shows the theological stakes of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر ما قيل فى الكبائر فإنها إذ صارت بحيث احتمال العفو فما دونها أولى وبما للقول به فيها على الاختلاف أثر بين فى الأمة ، فصرف الكلام إليه أحق ، وبالله التوفيق . ثم مسألة اختلاف المسلمين في مرتكبى الكبائر . اختلفت الأمة فى مرتكبى الكبائر من المسلمين ، دفعته إليها الغلبة من شهوة أو غفلة أو شدة الغضب والجمية أو رجاء العفو والتوبة من غير استحلال منه ولا استخفاف منه بمن أمر ونهى ، فمنهم من جعله كافرا ، ومنهم من جعله مشركا ، ومنهم من جعله غير مؤمن ولا كافر ، ومنهم من يجعله منافقا ، ومنهم من جعله مؤمنا على ما كان عاصيا بما فعل ، فاسقا به من غير أن يطلق له اسم الفسق والفجور إلا مع من يعلم ما به سمى ذلك ، ويرى أن يكون الله تعذيبه بقدر ذنبه والعفو عنه بما علم منه من الصدق له فى العبودة وغيره من الحسنات ، ومنهم من وقف في الوعيد إنه أريد به المستحيل أو غيره ، ورآه واجبا ، فتفريق من ذكرت بين الصغائر والكبائر فما يثبت فى الصغائر من إمكان العفو أو إبقاء الإيمان أوجب صرف الوعيد إلى الكبائر ، وما يثبت من ذكر جزاء الكفر والشرك ونحوه يوجب تحقيق اسم الشرك . وقول قوم : والكفر على قول ، وأيد ذلك قوله : " إِنَّهُ لَا يَيْأَسُ مِنْ رُوحِ اللَّهِ إِلَّا القَوْمُ الكَافِرُونَ " ٨٧ يوسف ، وقال : " وَمَنْ يَقْنَطُ مِنْ رَحْمَةِ رَبِّهِ إِلَّا الضَّالُّونَ " ٥٦ الحجر ، مع ما كان صاحب الكبيرة حاكما بغير الذى أنزل .
Then we mention what has been said about major sins, for since they have become subject to the possibility of forgiveness, then what is less than them is more so. And because the statement regarding them according to the difference has a clear effect in the community, directing the discussion to it is more appropriate. And with Allah is success. Then a question: The disagreement of Muslims regarding the perpetrators of major sins. The community disagreed concerning the perpetrators of major sins among Muslims—those whom overwhelming desire, heedlessness, intense anger and [؟]fierceness, or hope of forgiveness and repentance drove to them, without considering them lawful and without belittling the one who commands and forbids. Some considered him a disbeliever, some considered him a polytheist, some considered him neither a believer nor a disbeliever, some considered him a hypocrite, and some considered him a believer as before, though disobedient in what he did, a sinner (fasiq) thereby, without applying to him the name of sinfulness (fisq) and immorality (fujur) except with one who knows what he is called by that. And he holds that Allah will punish him according to his sin and forgive him according to what He knows of his sincerity in servitude and other good deeds. And some suspended judgment regarding the threat (al-wa'id) [of punishment], whether it is intended for the impossible or other than that, and considered it incumbent. Thus the distinction I mentioned between minor and major sins: what is established regarding minor sins of the possibility of forgiveness or the preservation of faith necessitates directing the threat to major sins. And what is established regarding the recompense of disbelief and polytheism and the like necessitates the realization of the name of polytheism. And some people say: 'and disbelief according to a saying,' and that is supported by His statement: 'Indeed, no one despairs of the spirit of Allah except the disbelieving people' (Yusuf 87), and He said: 'And who despairs of the mercy of his Lord except the astray?' (Al-Hijr 56), along with the fact that the perpetrator of a major sin judges by other than what Allah has revealed.
Source pages p. 928

Commentary
This passage is a classification and enumeration of the various Muslim positions regarding those who commit major sins (kaba'ir). Maturidi begins by noting that since major sins are subject to the possibility of forgiveness, then lesser sins are even more so. He then states that the difference of opinion on this matter is significant in the community, so it is appropriate to discuss it. He then presents the disagreement (ikhtilaf) of the Muslim community concerning those who commit major sins, driven by overwhelming desire, heedlessness, intense anger, or hope of forgiveness and repentance, without considering the sin lawful or belittling the divine command. The positions enumerated are: (1) some say he is a disbeliever (kafir), (2) some say he is a polytheist (mushrik), (3) some say he is neither a believer nor a disbeliever (i.e., the Mu'tazila position of a 'manzila bayn al-manzilatayn'), (4) some say he is a hypocrite (munafiq), (5) some say he remains a believer but is a disobedient sinner (fasiq), without applying the name fisq or fujur except with knowledge, and that God will punish him according to his sin and forgive him according to his sincerity and good deeds. (6) Some suspend judgment (waqf) regarding the divine threat (al-wa'id) — whether it refers to the impossible or something else — and consider it obligatory. Maturidi then draws a distinction: what is established regarding minor sins (possibility of forgiveness, preservation of faith) necessitates directing the threat to major sins. And what is established regarding the recompense of disbelief and polytheism necessitates realizing the name of polytheism for those who commit major sins (according to some views). Finally, he cites Quranic verses (Yusuf 87 and Al-Hijr 56) to support the view that despairing of God's mercy is a characteristic of disbelievers and the astray, implying that those who despair of forgiveness for major sinners may be in error. He also mentions that the perpetrator of a major sin judges by other than what Allah has revealed (a reference to those who rule by man-made laws).

ikhtilaf (disagreement)
kafir
mushrik

wa'id (threat)
despair

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi endorses a single position among those enumerated.
Correction: Maturidi objectively presents the various views without explicitly choosing one here; he is surveying the diversity of opinion.

Key Takeaway
The early Muslim community held at least six distinct views on the status of those who commit major sins, reflecting a deep theological diversity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Think of a fellow Muslim who commits a major sin openly. Do you still consider them a believer? If not, what does that say about your understanding of faith and forgiveness?
?
Maturidi mentions that some major sinners act out of hope for repentance and forgiveness. Do you ever use God's mercy as an excuse to delay repentance? How can you sincerely repent today?

The Disagreement on the Status of Major Sinners
Maturidi enumerates the differing views on the status of those who commit major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various theological positions held by early Muslim scholars on the status of those who commit major sins, as enumerated by Imam al-Maturidi.

Core Idea
Major sinners are neither uniformly condemned nor excused — multiple nuanced views exist.

Imam al-Maturidi opens this discussion by noting that if major sins can be forgiven, minor sins are even more likely to be pardoned. He then presents the key disagreement among Muslims regarding the status of a person who commits a major sin without considering it lawful. This passage enumerates the different positions held by various groups, showing the diversity of early Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the positions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay attention to the list of positions Maturidi presents. Try to count how many distinct views he mentions.
Look for phrases like 'منهم من جعله' (among them are those who considered him...).
2
Connect each position to known sects
Recall that Kharijites held the view of takfir, Mu'tazilites considered the sinner in an intermediate state, Murji'ah considered them believers despite sin, etc. Match each position to a sect or theological tendency known in early Islam.
This connection will help you understand the historical context.
3
Reflect on underlying assumptions
Consider what each position implies about the relationship between faith and works, and about God's justice and mercy. Why would someone adopt a particular stance?
Maturidi's own view is not explicit here; he is surveying.
4
Appreciate the significance of Maturidi's enumeration
Recognize that Maturidi's presentation shows the complexity of the issue and that early theological debates were multifaceted, not simply binary.
This passage sets the stage for Maturidi's own resolution later in the text.

ثم نذكر ما قيل فى الكبائر فإنها إذ صارت بحيث احتمال العفو فما دونها أولى وبما للقول به فيها على الاختلاف أثر بين فى الأمة ، فصرف الكلام إليه أحق ، وبالله التوفيق ثم مسألة اختلاف المسلمين في مرتكبى الكبائر اختلفت الأمة فى مرتكبى الكبائر من المسلمين ، دفعته إليها الغلبة من شهوة أو غفلة أو شدة الغضب والجمية أو رجاء العفو والتوبة من غير استحلال منه ولا استخفاف منه بمن أمر ونهى ، فمنهم من جعله كافرا ، ومنهم من جعله مشركا ، ومنهم من جعله غير مؤمن ولا كافر ، ومنهم من يجعله منافقا ، ومنهم من جعله مؤمنا على ما كان عاصيا بما فعل ، فاسقا به من غير أن يطلق له اسم الفسق والفجور إلا مع من يعلم ما به سمى ذلك ، ويرى أن يكون الله تعذيبه بقدر ذنبه والعفو عنه بما علم منه من الصدق له فى العبودة وغيره من الحسنات ، ومنهم من وقف في الوعيد إنه أريد به المستحيل أو غيره ، ورآه واجبا .
Then we mention what has been said concerning major sins (al-kabā'ir). For when they become such that forgiveness is possible, then what is less than them is more fitting. This passage is a classification. Maturidi introduces the topic of major sins and the disagreement among Muslims. He first notes the relevance of discussing minor sins, then presents the issue. He lists the various positions held by different groups: some consider the major sinner a disbeliever, some a polytheist, some neither, some a hypocrite, some a believer despite sin, some suspend judgment on the threat. Each position reflects different theological commitments regarding faith, sin, and divine punishment. The enumeration shows the complexity of early Islamic thought on this issue.

Refutation of the Mu'tazila's Denial of the Name Kufr
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila and criticizes two groups for mishandling names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the Mu'tazila's denial of the name 'kufr' and his critique of both the Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila for mishandling sacred names.

Core Idea
Names do not change realities; the Mu'tazila contradict themselves by affirming kufr's punishment but denying its name.

In this passage, Maturidi confronts a theological inconsistency: the Mu'tazila assign the full punishment of disbelief to major sinners yet refuse to call them disbelievers. He then widens his critique to two groups—the Hashwiyya and the Mu'tazila—who, in different ways, mishandle the names 'iman' and 'kufr', thereby undermining the weight of these terms in the hearts of believers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradiction
Read the passage and notice how Maturidi points out that the Mu'tazila are forced into a contradiction by affirming the punishment of kufr but denying the name.
The contradiction is that they affirm the same punishment yet avoid the label.
2
Understand the two groups' errors
The passage criticizes two groups: the Hashwiyya overextend 'iman' to every good deed, and the Mu'tazila withhold 'kufr' from major sinners. See how these are opposite mishandlings.
3
Reflect on the nature of names
Maturidi argues that names have no intrinsic benefit or harm; only realities matter. Consider why this matters for theology.
This connects to the core idea that names do not alter realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وألزموا الوعيد بما في الرفع لحوق الكذب، والله يجل عن ذلك، وكل الذى ذكرت يلزم المعتزلة فى منعهم تسمية الكفر. على أن قولين من أقاويل منتحلى الإسلام حصلا فى حق الاسماء على عبث وإبطال ما جبل عليه البشر من جلالة قدر الإيمان في قلوبهم، وعظم الله دين الإسلام في العقول، فصير أحد فريقى الإسلام اسم الإيمان لكل خير يقطع فزع تبدل دين الإسلام، وأزال جلالة قدره حيث أشركوا فى اسمه كل شيء مما يحتمل أن يكون له اسم الخير. فاشترك في هذا الحشوية والمعتزلة، وانفردت المعتزلة بمنع اسم الكفر عن أصحاب الكبائر، على تحقيق جميع ما في الكفر من العقوبة في ذلك، فلم يحصل لهم بما تحرجوا عن التسمية بما كان فزعهم عن اسمه إلا لعظيم الوعيد في ذلك، وإلا التسمية إذ لنفع يرجى أو لضرر يتقى، فكانت من المسمين بها إباحة إن ساءت أو حسنت إذا لم تكن يجب بحسنها حسن أو قبح، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And they required the threat in that which involves the attachment of lying, and Allah is exalted above that. And all that I have mentioned necessarily applies to the Mu'tazila in their prohibition of naming disbelief. Indeed, two opinions from the sayings of the claimants to Islam have occurred concerning names, amounting to frivolity and nullification of what human nature has been created upon: the majesty of the status of faith in their hearts, and the greatness of the religion of Islam in intellects. So one of the two groups of Islam made the name of faith for every good thing that prevents the fear of changing the religion of Islam, and removed the majesty of its status by associating in its name everything that could possibly have the name of good. So the Hashwiyya and the Mu'tazila share in this, and the Mu'tazila are unique in prohibiting the name of disbelief for the committers of major sins, while affirming all the punishment of disbelief in that. So they did not gain by their caution concerning the naming, except that their fear of that name was due to the great threat therein; otherwise, naming is only for a hoped benefit or a feared harm. So it is, with those who are named by them, an allowance whether it is bad or good, because it is not necessary that by its goodness there is goodness or by its ugliness there is ugliness. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 930

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's stance. Maturidi begins by stating that the Mu'tazila, by denying the name kufr to major sinners while affirming the same punishment, are forced into a contradiction (attaching lying to God). Then he condemns two extreme positions on naming among Muslims: the Hashwiyya extend the name 'iman' to any good act, diluting its majesty; the Mu'tazila uniquely withhold the name 'kufr' from major sinners out of fear of the threat, even though they acknowledge the punishment is identical. Maturidi argues that names have no intrinsic benefit or harm; only the realities matter. Their caution is pointless because naming does not change the punishment. He concludes that naming is merely an allowance based on human interests, not a determinant of moral value.

Key Terms
معتزلة
حشوية
اسم الإيمان
اسم الكفر
وعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Mu'tazila are consistent in their theology.
Correction: Maturidi shows they are contradictory: they affirm the punishment of kufr but deny the name, which is logically inconsistent.

Checkpoint
What error does Maturidi attribute to both the Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila together?
A) They both deny the name kufr for major sinners.
B) They both mishandle names, either by overextending 'iman' or by withholding 'kufr' despite affirming the punishment.
C) They both affirm the punishment of major sinners is less than that of disbelievers.
D) They both reject the importance of names entirely.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi exposes the inconsistency of the Mu'tazila who deny the name kufr but affirm its punishment, and criticizes both Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila for mishandling names.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there sins in your life that you hesitate to call by their true name—like 'disbelief', 'hypocrisy', or 'rebellion'—because calling them that would demand a radical change you are not ready to make? What would it cost you to name them honestly before Allah?
?
When you avoid the word 'kufr' for a major sin (or soften it to 'weakness' or 'mistake'), are you protecting your own comfort or the truth? Maturidi says naming is only for benefit or harm: whose benefit are you serving by your silence?
?
If you were to apply Maturidi's logic to your own heart—where have you overextended the name 'iman' to soothe yourself into believing routine acts of piety make you righteous? Can you face the gap between what you call yourself and what you truly are?

The Error of Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila in Naming Faith and Disbelief
Maturidi critiques the misuse of names for faith and disbelief by two sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi critiques the Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila for misusing the terms 'faith' and 'disbelief', and why their naming practices undermine the reverence due to faith.

Core Idea
Misusing the names of faith and disbelief distorts their true status and undermines the religion.

Maturidi exposes a paradox: While both sects claim to uphold Islam, their naming conventions for faith and disbelief actually devalue the very concepts. In this passage, he argues that the Hashwiyya trivialize faith by applying it too broadly, while the Mu'tazila withhold 'disbelief' from major sinners out of fear, yet still affirm the punishment—an unstable position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read Maturidi's Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own critique come through.
Notice the strong language: 'futility and nullification of human nature'.
2
Identify the two errors
Pinpoint how the Hashwiyya misuse 'faith' (extending it to every good deed) and how the Mu'tazila misuse 'disbelief' (refusing it to major sinners).
Both share a common error regarding the name of faith.
3
Reflect on the consequences
Ask yourself: Why does Maturidi consider these errors a nullification of the natural reverence for faith? Consider the implications for the exalted status of faith.
The threat (wa'id) of punishment is central to Mu'tazila's reluctance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وألزموا الوعيد بما في الرفع لحوق الكذب ، والله يجل عن ذلك ، وكل الذى ذكرت يلزم المعتزلة فى منعهم تسمية الكفر . على أن قولين من أقاويل منتحلى الإسلام حصلا فى حق الاسماء على عبث وإبطال ما جبل عليه البشر من جلالة قدر الإيمان في قلوبهم ، وعظم الله دين الإسلام في العقول ، فصير أحد فريقى الإسلام اسم الإيمان لكل خير يقطع فزع تبدل دين الإسلام ، وأزال جلالة قدره حيث أشركوا فى اسمه كل شيء مما يحتمل أن يكون له اسم الخير . فاشترك في هذا الحشوية والمعتزلة ، وانفردت المعتزلة بمنع اسم الكفر عن أصحاب الكبائر ، على تحقيق جميع ما في الكفر من العقوبة في ذلك ، فلم يحصل لهم بما تحرجوا عن التسمية بما كان فزعهم عن اسمه إلا لعظيم الوعيد في ذلك ، وإلا التسمية إذ لنفع يرجى أو لضرر يتقى ، فكانت من المسمين بها إباحة إن ساءت أو حسنت إذا لم تكن يجب بحسنها حسن أو قبح ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And they necessitated the threat (al-wa‘īd) because raising [the name] entails lying, and God is exalted above that. And everything I mentioned applies to the Mu‘tazila in their prohibition of naming disbelief (kufr). For two sayings among the sayings of those who claim Islam occurred concerning names, [resulting] in futility and nullification of what human nature was created upon: the exaltedness of the status of faith (īmān) in their hearts, and the greatness of the religion of Islam in minds. So one of the two groups of Islam made the name of faith for every good that removes fear of change of the religion of Islam, and removed the exaltedness of its status when they associated in its name everything that can have the name of good. Thus the Hashwiyya and the Mu‘tazila shared in this, and the Mu‘tazila were unique in prohibiting the name of disbelief from the committers of major sins, upon the realization of all the punishment of disbelief in that. So they did not gain, by their reluctance about naming, what was their fear of its name except for the great threat in that, and [they did not gain] the naming, since [it is] for anticipated benefit or feared harm. So it [the naming] was, among those who name, a permission, whether it is bad or good, since its goodness does not necessitate good or evil. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 930

Commentary
This passage is a critique of how two Islamic sects—the Hashwiyya and the Mu‘tazila—handle the naming of faith and disbelief. Maturidi begins by stating that the Mu‘tazila, by prohibiting the name of disbelief for sinners, are compelled into a position that implies lying about God, which is impossible for God. He then explains that there are two erroneous opinions among claimants of Islam regarding names: one group (Hashwiyya) extends the name of faith to every good deed, thereby trivializing its exalted status; the other (Mu‘tazila) withholds the name of disbelief from major sinners. Both, he says, undermine the natural reverence for faith and the greatness of Islam. The Hashwiyya and Mu‘tazila share the error of misusing the name of faith, but the Mu‘tazila uniquely refuse to call major sinners disbelievers, despite recognizing that such sinners deserve the punishment of disbelief. Maturidi points out that their fear of the threat (wa‘īd) leads them to avoid the name, but this avoidance does not truly benefit them, because naming is either for benefit or fear; if a name is simply permissible without moral consequence, the issue is not resolved. The passage ends with the standard formula 'There is no power except with God'.

Correction: Maturidi shows that their avoidance is tied to a theological stance on the threat (wa'īd) and the naming itself, not mere caution.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that misusing the names of faith and disbelief—whether by overextension or withholding—destroys the natural reverence for faith and undermines the religion.

?

Mu'tazila's Argument: The Major Sinner is Neither Believer nor Disbeliever
Maturidi presents the Mu'tazila's reasoning for not calling a major sinner a believer or disbeliever.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's reasoning for why a major sinner cannot be called a believer or a disbeliever, and how they arrived at the term 'fasiq' (transgressor).

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila placed the major sinner in a middle category: neither believer nor disbeliever, but fasiq.

In this passage, Maturidi presents the Mu'tazila's own argument for classifying the major sinner. They draw on the concepts of promise (wa'd) and threat (wa'id) to argue that a believer receives promise of paradise, while a major sinner incurs a threat of punishment, so he cannot be a believer. Yet Scripture does not call him a disbeliever, so they invent a third category: fasiq (transgressor).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
The Mu'tazila argue that the major sinner is called with bad names, and since faith is a good name associated with promise, the major sinner cannot be a believer. Also, Scripture does not call him a disbeliever.
2
Understand the two premises: faith is a good name, and promise (wa'd) is attached to it
The promise of paradise is tied to the name 'believer' and cannot be specified or limited. Therefore, a major sinner, who incurs threat, cannot be a believer.
3
See why they deny the name 'disbeliever'
The name 'disbeliever' was not intended for the major sinner (i.e., he is not outside Islam), so he cannot be called kafir.
This is a key point distinguishing Mu'tazila from Kharijites.
4
Notice the two arguments about the threat (generality and the verse)
First: threatening texts are general and apply to all major sinners. Second: Quran 4:31 distinguishes between major and minor sins, implying major sins are unforgivable without repentance, so they lead to eternal punishment.
They argue that eternal punishment (takhlid) is a greater deterrent.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The major sinner is called fasiq, fajir, zalim. The threat necessitates entry into Fire with no mention of exit, so the major sinner abides eternally in Hell.
This shows Mu'tazila's doctrine of eternal punishment for major sinners who die unrepentant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجت المعتزلة فى الاسم بما سمى صاحب الكبيرة باسماء خبيثة ، والإيمان من الأسماء الطيبة لا يسمى به ، مع ما جاء من الوعد باسم الإيمان ، والوعد لا يحتمل الخصوص، ثم صاحب الكبيرة قد جاء فيه الوعيد ، فبطل أن يكون مؤمنا ولم يسم به كافرا بما لم يرد به التسمية ، فسمى به الذي أجمع أنه له اسم وهو الفسق والفجور والظلم . ثم لهم فى الوعيد أمران : عموم أخباره ، والثاني قوله : إِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَائِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ ، بين ما لا يغفر ويغفر ، مع بالتخليد أعظم في المنع وأبلغ في الزجر فهو أحق ، على أن الوعيد إذا وجب لزم دخول النار ، ولم يذكر فيهم الخروج ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And the Mu‘tazila argued concerning the name, because the committer of a major sin is named with bad names, while faith is among the good names—he should not be named with it—along with what came of the promise (al-wa‘d) by the name of faith, and the promise does not admit specification. Then the committer of a major sin has the threat (al-wa‘īd) come concerning him, so it is invalid that he be a believer, and he is not named a disbeliever because the name [disbelief] was not intended for him. So he is named with that which there is consensus that it is his name: transgression (fisq), immorality (fujūr), and wrongdoing (ẓulm). Then they have two [aspects] concerning the threat: the generality of its reports, and the second is His saying: 'If you avoid the major sins which you are forbidden…' distinguishing between what is not forgiven and what is forgiven, while with eternity (al-takhlīd) it is greater in prevention and more efficient in deterrence, so it is more worthy. And [they argue] that if the threat is established, entry into the Fire is necessary, and no exit is mentioned for them. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 930

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu‘tazila’s argument in their own words, likely as a prelude to refutation. They reason: the committer of a major sin deserves bad names; faith is a good name and cannot be applied to him because the divine promise (wa‘d) of paradise is attached to the name 'believer,' and that promise cannot be specified or limited. Since the major sinner also has the threat (wa‘īd) of punishment, he cannot be called a believer. At the same time, he cannot be called a disbeliever because the name 'disbelief' was not intended for him (i.e., he is not outside Islam). So he is called by the agreed-upon name: fāsiq (transgressor), fājir (immoral), ẓālim (wrongdoer). Then they discuss two points about the threat: first, the general nature of the threatening texts (they apply to all sinners without discrimination); second, the verse (Q. 4:31) which distinguishes between major and minor sins, implying that major sins are unforgivable unless one repents. They conclude that eternal punishment (takhlīd) is more effective as a deterrent, and once the threat applies, it necessitates entering the Fire with no mention of exit (i.e., eternal punishment for the major sinner). The passage ends with the usual formula.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila consider major sinners as disbelievers (kuffar).
Correction: In fact, the Mu'tazila call them fasiqun (transgressors), not kafirun. They affirm the person is still part of the Muslim community but in a middle state.

The Mu'tazila argue that the major sinner cannot be called believer due to the promise, nor disbeliever for lack of scriptural designation, so they adopt the term fasiq and hold that the major sinner will be eternally punished in Hell.

Does that confidence come from God's promise or from your own assumption that you are still a believer despite your sin?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that the threat of eternal punishment for major sinners is a more effective deterrent. Do you think fear of punishment is what keeps you from sinning? And if you sin anyway, what does that say about your fear of God's threat?

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Claim: Despair Implies Disbelief
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazilite view that despair does not entail disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradiction in the Mu'tazilite position on grave sinners, based on the Qur'anic link between despair and disbelief and the definitions of kufr and iman.

Core Idea
To affirm despair of God's mercy is to affirm disbelief—the Qur'an binds them together.

The Mu'tazila held that a grave sinner is in a state of despair from God's mercy but refused to label such a person a disbeliever. Maturidi exposes the inconsistency in this position by citing the Qur'anic verse that explicitly states that only disbelievers despair of God's spirit. This passage shows how a single scriptural proof can dismantle a whole theological stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp Maturidi's argument.
Pay attention to the Qur'anic citation and how it is used.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim and Maturidi's objection
Note the Mu'tazilite position: affirm despair but deny disbelief. Maturidi calls this contradictory because the Qur'an links despair with disbelief.
Think: why is it contradictory?
3
Examine Maturidi's dual argument
First argument: kufr as denial (takdhīb) – the sinner is not a denier. Second argument: kufr as covering (satr) – the sinner does not cover God's blessings. Thus he is not a kāfir.
Notice how Maturidi uses both custom (ʿurf) and linguistic reality (ḥaqīqa).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the grave sinner is a believer because iman is affirmation (taṣdīq) and he has not denied anything of God.
How does this refute the Mu'tazilite position?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قول المعتزلة في تحقيق الإياس من روح الله مع نفيهم اسم الكفر، وقد قال الله تعالى: إِنَّهُ لَا يَيْأَسُ مِنْ رُوحِ اللَّهِ إِلَّا الْقَوْمُ الْكَافِرُونَ، قول متناقض جمع بين الكفر والإياس، فمن أثبت أحدهما لزم الآخر. فإذ ثبت عندنا أنه ليس بكافر: إذ الكفر في العرف تكذيب، وصاحب الكبيرة بالتصديق في حالة يرجو عفو ربه ويخاف عذابه، ويعلم أن أيأسه من ربه ضال جاهل بالله، ثبت أنه ليس بمكذب. وفي الحقيقة الكفر اسم للستر وصاحبه لا يستر شيئا من نعم ربه ولا ينكر حقه، فيبطل أن يكون كافرا، فمثله الإيمان في العرف والسمع تصديق. ومعلوم أنه لم يكذب الله في شيء، ثبت أنه مؤمن، والله الموفق.
Then the saying of the Mu'tazila about affirming despair from the spirit of Allah while they deny the name of disbelief, and Allah the Most High said: 'Indeed, no one despairs of the spirit of Allah except the disbelieving people.' A contradictory saying that combines disbelief and despair, so whoever affirms one of them, the other necessarily follows. So when it is established with us that he is not a disbeliever: since disbelief in custom is denial, and the committer of a major sin is in a state of affirming [the truth], hoping for the pardon of his Lord and fearing His punishment, and knows that his despair of his Lord is misguided and ignorant of Allah, it is established that he is not a denier. And in reality, disbelief is a name for covering, and its possessor does not cover any of his Lord's blessings nor deny His right, so it is invalid that he be a disbeliever. Similarly, faith in custom and in the revealed law is affirmation. And it is known that he has not denied Allah in anything, so it is established that he is a believer. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 933

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite position regarding the grave sinner. The Mu'tazila claimed that the grave sinner is in a state of 'despair' (iyās) from God's mercy, but they still denied that the sinner becomes a disbeliever (kāfir). Maturidi points out that the Qur'ān (Sūrat Yūsuf 12:87) states that only disbelievers despair of the spirit (rawḥ) of Allah. Therefore, the Mu'tazilite claim is contradictory: if they affirm despair, they must also affirm disbelief, because despair is a property of disbelief. Conversely, if they deny disbelief, they must deny despair. Maturidi then argues that the grave sinner is not a disbeliever because disbelief (kufr) in common usage means 'denial' (takdhīb), while the grave sinner, who affirms faith, hopes for God's pardon, fears His punishment, and knows that despair would be ignorance, is not a denier. Furthermore, linguistically, kufr means 'covering' (satr), and the grave sinner does not cover God's blessings or deny His rights. Therefore, he is not a kāfir. Similarly, faith (īmān) in both common usage and revealed law means 'affirmation' (taṣdīq), and since the sinner has not denied anything from Allah, he remains a believer.

Key Terms

روح الله
كفر
تكذيب
ستر
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Despair from God's mercy is a lesser sin than disbelief and does not necessarily imply disbelief.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the Qur'an (12:87) directly links despair with disbelief: 'Indeed, no one despairs of the spirit of Allah except the disbelieving people.' Therefore, affirming despair entails affirming disbelief.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say the Mu'tazilite position on the grave sinner is contradictory?
A) They affirm faith but deny despair.
B) They affirm despair but deny the name of disbelief, while the Qur'an links despair with disbelief.
C) They claim the sinner is a believer and a disbeliever simultaneously.
D) They deny both despair and disbelief.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Mu'tazilite position on the grave sinner is self-contradictory because the Qur'an inextricably links despair with disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your sin is so great that God's mercy cannot reach you? This lesson tells you that such despair is itself a sign of disbelief. Will you tonight, in the quiet of your heart, reject that despair and proclaim hope in God's forgiveness as an act of faith?
?
When you sin, do you quickly fall into hopelessness, thinking God has abandoned you? The Mu'tazilites tried to separate despair from disbelief, but Maturidi shows they are inseparable. Is there a sin you have stopped praying for forgiveness for? Will you commit to asking for mercy for that one sin this week?
?
This lesson defines faith as affirmation (tasdiq) — not just words, but a state of the heart that hopes and fears. Do your hopes and fears about your afterlife truly affirm God's mercy and justice, or do they betray a hidden despair? What one thought pattern about God's treatment of you must you change starting now?

Final Ruling on Kharijites and Mu'tazilites
Maturidi gives a final ruling on two sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi declares the Kharijites and Mu'tazilites to be disbelievers—not merely as grave sinners, but because their doctrine of declaring others disbelievers is itself a form of kufr.

Core Idea
Declaring others disbelievers based on one's own error is itself disbelief.

After a detailed argument on faith and grave sins, Imam Maturidi delivers his final ruling: the Kharijites and Mu'tazilites, because they insist that grave sinners are disbelievers, are themselves disbelievers. This short but sharp conclusion underscores that the error lies not in committing sin, but in the very judgment that removes faith from sinners. The passage is fragmentary but the decisive point is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the incomplete ending—this is due to a damaged manuscript. The key phrase is 'all the Kharijites and Mu'tazilites... are disbelievers.'
The garbled ending 'upon the wealth except it was from him' is likely a scribal error or damaged text.
2
Identify the ruling
Maturidi states 'the truth is to be said'—he is giving a decisive ruling. He applies the term 'disbelievers' (kuffar) to the Kharijites and Mu'tazilites when they commit major sins. But note: it is not the sin itself that makes them disbelievers, but their doctrinal position.
The commentary explains that their doctrine—denying that faith remains with the sinner—leads to kufr.
3
Reflect on why this ruling
Consider why Maturidi takes such a strong stance. The Kharijites declared grave sinners to be disbelievers; the Mu'tazilites considered them in an intermediate state. Both views reject the consensus that faith can coexist with sin. By denying this consensus, they effectively reject clear proofs—and that rejection is itself kufr.
This ruling is not about individuals committing sins, but about the doctrine itself.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Recall that earlier in the book, Maturidi argued that the name of faith remains upon the sinner. This passage concludes that those who oppose that view are outside Islam. It is a strong boundary-marking statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الحق أن يقال: جميع الخوارج والمعتزلة عند ارتكابهم الكبائر كفرة على [؟] المال إلا كانت منه [؟]
Then the truth is to be said: All the Kharijites and Mu'tazilites, when they commit major sins, are disbelievers upon [؟] the wealth except it was from him [؟]
Source pages p. 933

Commentary
This passage gives the final ruling. Maturidi states that the truth is to declare all Kharijites (khawārij) and Mu'tazilites (muʿtazilah) as disbelievers (kuffār) when they commit major sins. This is because their doctrine itself is based on rejecting the clear proofs that faith remains with the sinner, and their position (that grave sinners are disbelievers or in a state between belief and disbelief) leads to kufr. The text is incomplete, but the meaning is clear: those who hold such erroneous views are themselves disbelievers because they deny the consensus and scriptural evidence that the name of faith remains upon the sinner.

Key Terms
خوارج
معتزلة
كفرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Maturidi's ruling means that all grave sinners are disbelievers, or that the Kharijites and Mu'tazilites are disbelievers simply because they commit major sins.
Correction: Maturidi's ruling is specifically about their doctrine: they are disbelievers because they declare others disbelievers based on sin, not because they themselves sin. The sin is not the cause of their kufr; their false belief is.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, whom does Maturidi declare to be disbelievers?
A) All grave sinners.
B) The Kharijites and Mu'tazilites who hold that grave sinners leave faith.
C) Only the Kharijites.
D) Only the Mu'tazilites who deny intercession.

Key Takeaway
Declaring others disbelievers based on a false doctrine itself constitutes disbelief, and this is why Maturidi rules against the Kharijites and Mu'tazilites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered someone's faith to be invalid because of a sin they committed? How does Maturidi's ruling that such a judgment itself can be disbelief challenge your own tendency to judge others?
?
Think of a theological disagreement you have with someone. What would it mean for you if your error in that matter could be as grave as the error of the Kharijites? Does that humble you?
?
When you hear about a sect or group that differs from you, do you immediately dismiss them as disbelievers? After this lesson, what would you need to examine in yourself before making such a judgment?

The Contradiction of Despair and Disbelief
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazila by showing their position entails despair.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite position on major sinners is self-contradictory according to Maturidi's argument from the Quran.

Core Idea
Despair of God's mercy and disbelief are inseparable; you cannot have one without the other.

Maturidi takes aim at the Mu'tazilite claim that a major sinner is in an intermediate state, neither believer nor disbeliever. He points out that they still consider the sinner to be in despair of God's mercy, which the Quran explicitly attributes only to disbelievers. In this passage, he exposes the logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: statement of Mu'tazila position, Quranic quote, and conclusion.
The passage is short but packed with logic.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite position
What do the Mu'tazila say about the major sinner? They say he is in an intermediate state (not a believer, not a disbeliever). But Maturidi highlights that they still affirm 'despair of God's mercy' (iyas) for him.
The key is they deny the name of kufr but affirm the attribute of despair.
3
Understand the Quranic evidence
Maturidi quotes Quran 12:87: 'Indeed, no one despairs of the relief of Allah except the disbelieving people.' This ties despair directly to disbelief.
The verse makes despair a characteristic of kuffar.
4
Grasp the logical conclusion
If despair entails disbelief, then affirming despair requires affirming disbelief. The Mu'tazila cannot have it both ways. Whoever affirms one, the other necessarily follows.
This is a straightforward logical refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قول المعتزلة في تحقيق الإياس من روح الله مع نفيهم اسم الكفر، وقد قال الله تعالى: إِنَّهُ لَا يَيْأَسُ مِنْ رَوْحِ اللَّهِ إِلَّا الْقَوْمُ الْكَافِرُونَ، قول متناقض جمع بين الكفر والإياس، فمن أثبت أحدهما لزم الآخر. فإذ ثبت عندنا أنه ليس بكافر: إذ الكفر في العرف تكذيب، وصاحب الكبيرة مصدق [؟] بالتصديق في حالة يرجو عفو ربه ويخاف عذابه، ويعلم أن أيأسه من ربه ضلال وجهل بالله، ثبت أنه ليس بمكذب. وفي الحقيقة الكفر اسم للستر، وصاحبه لا يستر شيئا من نعم ربه ولا ينكر حقه، فيبطل أن يكون كافرا. فمثله الإيمان في العرف والسمع تصديق. ومعلوم أنه لم يكذب الله في شيء، ثبت أنه مؤمن، والله الموفق.
Then the saying of the Mu'tazila in affirming despair of the spirit of Allah while they deny the name of disbelief—and Allah Most High said: 'Indeed, no one despairs of the relief of Allah except the disbelieving people'—is a contradictory saying that combines disbelief and despair; whoever affirms one of them, the other necessarily follows. Since it is established with us that he is not a disbeliever—for kufr in common usage is denial, and the committer of a major sin is a believer [؟] with affirmation in a state where he hopes for his Lord's pardon and fears His punishment, and knows that despair of his Lord is misguidance and ignorance of Allah—it is established that he is not a denier. And in reality, kufr is a name for covering; its possessor does not cover any of his Lord's blessings nor deny His right, so it is invalid that he be a disbeliever. Likewise, iman in common usage and revelation is affirmation. And it is known that he has not denied Allah in anything; it is established that he is a believer. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 933

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite position that a major sinner is neither a believer nor a disbeliever (the intermediate state). The author argues that their claim of not applying the name 'disbeliever' while simultaneously affirming despair (al-iyas) of God's mercy is self-contradictory, because the Quran (12:87) states that only disbelievers despair of God's mercy. If they affirm despair, they must affirm disbelief. But we know the sinner is not a disbeliever, because kufr (disbelief) in common usage (al-'urf) means denial (takdhib)—the sinner does not deny God, he affirms faith (tasdiq) while hoping for forgiveness and fearing punishment. Moreover, despair of God's mercy is itself ignorance and misguidance, which the sinner does not hold. In its literal sense, kufr means 'covering' (al-sitr), but the sinner does not cover God's blessings—he acknowledges them. Similarly, iman is affirmation (tasdiq) in both common usage and revelation; since the sinner affirms God, he is a believer. Thus the Mu'tazilite position collapses.

Key Terms
يأس
إياس
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazila think they can affirm despair of mercy without calling the sinner a kafir.
Correction: The Quran ties despair to disbelief, so affirming despair logically implies disbelief.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the Mu'tazilite position contradictory?
A) Because they deny the sinner any status
B) Because they affirm despair of God's mercy while denying disbelief
C) Because they claim the sinner is a believer
D) Because they reject the Quran entirely

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verse proves that despair of God's mercy is exclusive to disbelievers, making the Mu'tazilite position contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your sins are too great for Allah to forgive? How does this verse confront that feeling, and what must you change in your heart to replace despair with hope?
?
The Mu'tazila tried to create a middle ground, but the Quran is clear. Are you tempted to rationalize away clear Quranic truths to fit your own preferences? What would it cost you to submit fully to what Allah has said?
?
Imagine you are in a state of deep despair. According to this lesson, that despair itself is a form of disbelief. What would it take for you to truly trust in Allah's mercy even when you feel unworthy?
