Consensus on Naming the Major Sinner and Refutation of the Mu'tazila
Maturidi states the consensus on naming the major sinner and refutes those who deny these names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Maturidi, the Muslim community agreed that the major sinner has a religious designation, and the Mu'tazila innovated the terms fasiq and fajir to avoid calling them kafir or mushrik.

Core Idea
The major sinner is not left unnamed; the Mu'tazila's innovation contradicts consensus.

Imam Maturidi begins by affirming a point of consensus among all Muslims: the committer of a major sin is still identified by a religious name. He then criticizes those who abolish these names out of excessive caution, and specifically targets the Mu'tazila for inventing new labels to avoid applying the names of kufr and shirk to such a person. This lesson unpacks this argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the consensus
Read the first part of the passage. The community agreed that the major sinner has a name from the religions (shirk, kufr, Islam). This is a point of unity despite other disagreements.
Note that the consensus is about the existence of a name, not which specific name.
2
Consequence of abolishing names
Maturidi argues that anyone who abolishes these names out of caution against doubt has rejected the consensus and also the Book and Sunnah that affirm these names.
3
Dispute over fasiq and fajir
The passage then notes that the terms fasiq and fajir are disputed: some apply them, others call the major sinner kafir or mushrik, and others call him mu'min. This shows disagreement on the correct label.
The Mu'tazila are singled out for innovating fasiq/fajir to block kafir/mushrik.
4
Identify the Mu'tazila innovation
Maturidi accuses the Mu'tazila of introducing the two names (fasiq and fajir) specifically to prevent the application of kafir and mushrik. This contradicts the actual state of affairs.
Consider why this innovation is problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأمة على اختلافهم اتفقت على أن لصاحب الكبيرة اسما من الأديان من شرك أو كفر أو إسلام ، فمن أبطلها توقيا أن ينطق بالشك أبطل ما أجمع على القول به ، وشهدوا على مجيء الكتاب به والسنة بما لديه يرتفع الريب عمن تلقى السمع وهو شهيد أو له قلب ، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم القول بالفاسق والفاجر مطلقا مما يتوزع فيه ، ومن سماه كافرا أو مشركا أطلقه ، ومن سماه مؤمنا أبى ذلك ، وكذلك جحدوا اسم أعداء الله ، وأبدعت المعتزلة هذين الاسمين على منع ذينك الاسمين خلافا لما عليه الأمر ، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the community, despite their differences, agreed that the person of the major sin has a name from the religions, whether shirk, kufr, or Islam. Whoever abolishes these names out of caution against uttering doubt has abolished what they unanimously agreed to utter, and they testified that the Book and the Sunnah came with it, by which doubt is removed from one who hears with witness or has a heart. And there is no power except with Allah. Then the statement regarding the fasiq and the fajir absolutely is something disputed. Whoever calls him a disbeliever (kafir) or a polytheist (mushrik) has pronounced it, and whoever calls him a believer (mu'min) refused that. Likewise they denied the name of the enemies of Allah. And the Mu'tazila innovated these two names (i.e., fasiq and fajir) in order to prevent those two names (kafir and mushrik) contrary to what the matter is upon. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 936

Commentary
This passage is a statement of consensus and a refutation. It begins by affirming that the community, despite their differences, agreed that the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira) has a name derived from the religions, either shirk, kufr, or Islam. The term 'name from the religions' means that such a person is not left nameless but is designated according to his religious status. Then Maturidi argues that whoever abolishes these names out of fear of uttering doubt has actually abolished what the community unanimously agreed upon. The community testified that the Qur'an and Sunnah affirm these names, which removes doubt from those who listen with understanding. Then the discussion turns to the terms 'fasiq' and 'fajir' (sinner, transgressor). Some call the major sinner a disbeliever (kafir) or polytheist (mushrik), while others call him a believer (mu'min) and refuse the former. This shows disagreement. Then Maturidi accuses the Mu'tazila of innovating the terms fasiq and fajir in order to prevent applying the terms kafir and mushrik to the major sinner, which contradicts the actual state of affairs. The passage ends with a reminder that power is only from Allah.

Key Takeaway

Refuting Khawarij and Mu'tazila on Forgiveness of Major Sins
Maturidi refutes Khawarij and Mu'tazila on two forgiveness verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the Khawarij interpretation of verse 4:48 and the Mu'tazila interpretation of verse 4:31 regarding forgiveness of major sins.

Core Idea
Forgiveness of shirk requires repentance; other major sins can be expiated by good deeds.

Imam Maturidi engages with two sectarian interpretations that misapply Quranic verses on forgiveness. The Khawarij wrongly infer that shirk is like other sins, while the Mu'tazila claim that major sins are automatically forgiven through avoidance. In this passage, Maturidi exposes the flaws in their reasoning using the verses themselves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two verses discussed
Read the passage and note that Maturidi first addresses verse 4:48 (shirk) and then verse 4:31 (avoiding major sins).
2
Understand the Khawarij error
See how Maturidi argues that the Khawarij misunderstand the verse; shirk is not a sin that can be forgiven by mere avoidance.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila error
Notice that Maturidi rejects the Mu'tazila claim that major sins are forgiven without expiation, because the verse mentions expiation (takfir).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قوله : إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَنْ يُشْرَكَ بِهِ ، تأويل الخوارج فيه من الخطأ فاسد ؛ لأنه ليس بذنب فيغفر ، وفى هذا ذكر المغفرة ، ولا يحتمل إضمار التوبة لما يغفر بمثله الشرك ، والآية فى التمييز بين الذنبين ، وكذلك لا يحتمل قوله : إِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَائِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ ، ؛ لما فيه التكفير ، وما لا ذنب لا يكفر والخطأ لا يحقق الذنب ، والتكفير يكون لشيء يجز به ، ولا يحتمل ما قالت المعتزلة لأن قولهم يمنع تحقيق الشبه؛ إذ هي تقع من مجتنب الكبائر مغفورة ، وفى هذا اثباتها ، ثم التكفير ، وهم يجعلونها مغفور [0] لا مكفرة ؛ إذ المغفورة هى التى تستر عليها ، وفى بقائها إلى مدة دفعها ، والمكفره هي التي يأتى من صاحبها فعل حسن يكفر به نحو قوله : فَأُولَئِكَ يُبَدِّلُ اللهُ سَيِّئَاتِهِم حَسَنَاتٍ ، وقوله : هَلْ أَدُلُّكُمْ عَلَى تِجَارَةٍ ، وقوله : إِنْ تُبْدُوا الصَّدَقَاتِ ، إلى ذكر التكفير ، وكذلك قوله : تُوبُوا إِلَى اللهِ تَوْبَةً نَصُوحًا ، إِنَّ الحَسَنَاتِ يُذْهِبْنَ السَّيِّئَاتِ . وبعد فإن الآية لا تحتمل قول المعتزلة ؛ لما هم يجعلون المصر عنى ب صاحب ، ومن لا يصر عليه فهو تائب عنه ، نادم عليه ، وفي حث إنه يغفر بالتوبة ، وكل الذنوب تغفر بها . والإثنان جريا بالتفريق تدله : إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَنْ يُشْرَكَ بِه ، وقوله : إِنْ تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَائِرَ مَا تُبَ عَنْهُ ، فهو والله أعلم أن الشرك لا يغفر إلا بالتوبة عنه ، وغير [0] يجوز أو يكثر بغيره من الحسنات ليصح القول مع تحقيق الفائدة لتعيد قوة إلا بالله بالتفضل قرآن ، ولا ثم للآية وجوه تمنع قول المعتزلة والخوارج أحدها أنه قال : إِن تَحْسَبُوا كَبَائِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ ، وليس في ذلك بيان حكم من لا يجتنب . باتت أن الكبائر نوعان : أحدهما كبائر في الاعتقاد من أنواع الكفر والتكذيب التي بها اختلفت الكفرة ، وأخرى كبائر الأفعال التي صاحبها مجتنب عنها بالانتفخ في أن يراها على ما جعلها الله عليه من عظم الفعل والذنب ، وهذا اجتناب ، وقد وقعها بالفعل، فهو الارتكاب ، وليس فى الآية وجه الاجتناب ، فجائز أن تحمد : أن يجتنب كبائر الاعتقاد ، وهى أنواع الشرك يكفر عنها دونها لمن يشاء . ذلك من الحسنات أو بالتفضل كما بينا في إحدى الآيتين بالتحف وفي الأخرى بالمغفرة ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then His statement: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' (Quran 4:48). The interpretation of the Khawarij in it is corrupt error; because it is not a sin that is forgiven, and in this is the mention of forgiveness, and it does not admit the implication of repentance for that by which shirk is forgiven, and the verse is for distinguishing between the two sins. Likewise, His statement: 'If you avoid the major sins that you are forbidden from' (Quran 4:31) does not admit [what they say] because of the expiation [mentioned] in it, and what has no sin does not expiate, and error does not actualize sin, and expiation is for something by which one is recompensed. And it does not admit what the Mu'tazila said, because their statement prevents the realization of the likeness; since they occur from one who avoids major sins as forgiven, and in this is their establishment, then expiation, while they make them forgiven [?], not expiated; for the forgiven is that which is covered over, and in its remaining for a period is its repulsion, and the expiated is that which comes from its doer a good deed by which it is expiated, like His statement: 'Those, Allah will replace their evil deeds with good deeds' (Quran 25:70), and His statement: 'Shall I guide you to a trade' (Quran 61:10), and His statement: 'If you give charity openly' (Quran 2:271), to the mention of expiation, and likewise His statement: 'Repent to Allah with sincere repentance' (Quran 66:8), 'Indeed good deeds remove evil deeds' (Quran 11:114). And after, the verse does not admit the statement of the Mu'tazila, because they make the persistent [?] the owner, and whoever does not persist in it is repenting from it, regretful of it, and in incitement that He forgives with repentance, and all sins are forgiven by it. And the two [verses] proceed with differentiation indicating: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' and His statement: 'If you avoid the major sins that you are forbidden from', so He, and Allah knows best, that shirk is not forgiven except through repentance from it, and other [?] is permissible or increased by other than it from good deeds so that the statement is correct with the realization of benefit to return [?] power except with Allah by favor [of] Quran, and no. Then for the verse are aspects that prevent the statement of the Mu'tazila and the Khawarij. One of them is that He said: 'If you avoid the major sins that you are forbidden from', and in that is no clarification of the ruling of one who does not avoid. It became clear that major sins are of two types: one is major sins in belief from the types of disbelief and denial by which the disbelievers differed, and another is major sins of actions whose doer avoids them by avoidance [?] in that he considers them as Allah has made them upon him of greatness of action and sin, and this is avoidance, and he has committed them by action, so it is commission, and in the verse there is no aspect of avoidance, so it is possible that one is praised: that he avoids major sins of belief, and they are types of shirk, and He expiates for them other than them for whomever He wills. That is from good deeds or by favor as we explained in one of the two verses with expiation and in the other with forgiveness. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 936

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij interpretations of two Quranic verses concerning forgiveness and major sins. The author begins with the verse 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' (4:48). He states that the Khawarij interpretation is corrupt because they misunderstand the nature of shirk—it is not a sin that can be forgiven by something other than repentance; the verse mentions forgiveness to distinguish shirk from other sins. Next, he turns to the verse 'If you avoid the major sins that you are forbidden from' (4:31). He argues that this verse does not support the Mu'tazila claim because it mentions expiation (takfir), and expiation applies to something that has a sin, but error (kata') does not constitute a sin. The Mu'tazila position that major sins are forgiven without expiation is rejected because it would imply that sins remain covered (maghfura) without being expiated (mukaffara), whereas the Quran shows that good deeds can expiate sins (e.g., 25:70, 61:10, 2:271, 66:8, 11:114). The author then clarifies that the two verses together show a distinction: shirk requires repentance, but other major sins may be forgiven through good deeds or divine favor (tafaddul). He then provides further analysis: the verse about avoiding major sins does not specify the ruling for one who does not avoid them, so it is inferred that major sins are of two types—those of belief (kufr) and those of actions. The avoidance of major sins in belief is praised, and such sins (shirk) can be expiated for others (i.e., minor sins) by good deeds or divine favor, as explained in the two verses. The passage concludes with a reminder of dependence on Allah.

كبيرة
مغفرة
تكفير
توبة
معتزلة
خوارج
شرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because they claim shirk is not a sin
B) Because they ignore the mention of repentance
C) Because they think shirk can be forgiven like other sins
D) Because they misinterpret the word 'forgive'

Key Takeaway
Shirk requires repentance for forgiveness, while other major sins involve expiation through good deeds.

Maturidi's Positive Doctrine: Forgiveness of Major Sins Beyond Shirk
Maturidi explains his own view on forgiveness of major sins and categorizes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's positive teaching that major sins other than shirk can be forgiven through good deeds or divine favor, and the distinction between two types of major sins.

Core Idea
Good deeds can expiate major sins, but shirk requires repentance.

After refuting the errors of the Khawarij and Mu'tazila, Maturidi presents his own understanding of the two Quranic verses. He clarifies that shirk is only forgiven through repentance, while other major sins may be expiated by good deeds or divine mercy. He also categorizes major sins into those of belief and those of actions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction between shirk and other sins
Note that Maturidi emphasizes that shirk is not forgiven except through repentance, while other major sins can be forgiven by good deeds or divine favor.
2
Learn the two categories of major sins
Identify that Maturidis divide major sins into those related to belief (kufr) and those related to actions. Avoidance of belief-related major sins is praised.
3
Connect to Quranic evidence
Observe how Maturidi uses verses like 25:70 (replacing evil with good) and 11:114 (good deeds remove evil) to support his position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قوله : إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَنْ يُشْرَكَ بِهِ ، تأويل الخوارج فيه من الخطأ فاسد ؛ لأنه ليس بذنب فيغفر ، وفى هذا ذكر المغفرة ، ولا يحتمل إضمار التوبة لما يغفر بمثله الشرك ، والآية فى التمييز بين الذنبين ، وكذلك لا يحتمل قوله : إِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَائِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ ، ؛ لما فيه التكفير ، وما لا ذنب لا يكفر والخطأ لا يحقق الذنب ، والتكفير يكون لشيء يجز به ، ولا يحتمل ما قالت المعتزلة لأن قولهم يمنع تحقيق الشبه؛ إذ هي تقع من مجتنب الكبائر مغفورة ، وفى هذا اثباتها ، ثم التكفير ، وهم يجعلونها مغفور [0] لا مكفرة ؛ إذ المغفورة هى التى تستر عليها ، وفى بقائها إلى مدة دفعها ، والمكفره هي التي يأتى من صاحبها فعل حسن يكفر به نحو قوله : فَأُولَئِكَ يُبَدِّلُ اللهُ سَيِّئَاتِهِم حَسَنَاتٍ ، وقوله : هَلْ أَدُلُّكُمْ عَلَى تِجَارَةٍ ، وقوله : إِنْ تُبْدُوا الصَّدَقَاتِ ، إلى ذكر التكفير ، وكذلك قوله : تُوبُوا إِلَى اللهِ تَوْبَةً نَصُوحًا ، إِنَّ الحَسَنَاتِ يُذْهِبْنَ السَّيِّئَاتِ . وبعد فإن الآية لا تحتمل قول المعتزلة ؛ لما هم يجعلون المصر عنى ب صاحب ، ومن لا يصر عليه فهو تائب عنه ، نادم عليه ، وفي حث إنه يغفر بالتوبة ، وكل الذنوب تغفر بها . والإثنان جريا بالتفريق تدله : إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَنْ يُشْرَكَ بِه ، وقوله : إِنْ تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَائِرَ مَا تُبَ عَنْهُ ، فهو والله أعلم أن الشرك لا يغفر إلا بالتوبة عنه ، وغير [0] يجوز أو يكثر بغيره من الحسنات ليصح القول مع تحقيق الفائدة لتعيد قوة إلا بالله بالتفضل قرآن ، ولا ثم للآية وجوه تمنع قول المعتزلة والخوارج أحدها أنه قال : إِن تَحْسَبُوا كَبَائِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ ، وليس في ذلك بيان حكم من لا يجتنب . باتت أن الكبائر نوعان : أحدهما كبائر في الاعتقاد من أنواع الكفر والتكذيب التي بها اختلفت الكفرة ، وأخرى كبائر الأفعال التي صاحبها مجتنب عنها بالانتفخ في أن يراها على ما جعلها الله عليه من عظم الفعل والذنب ، وهذا اجتناب ، وقد وقعها بالفعل، فهو الارتكاب ، وليس فى الآية وجه الاجتناب ، فجائز أن تحمد : أن يجتنب كبائر الاعتقاد ، وهى أنواع الشرك يكفر عنها دونها لمن يشاء . ذلك من الحسنات أو بالتفضل كما بينا في إحدى الآيتين بالتحف وفي الأخرى بالمغفرة ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then His statement: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' (Quran 4:48). The interpretation of the Khawarij in it is corrupt error; because it is not a sin that is forgiven, and in this is the mention of forgiveness, and it does not admit the implication of repentance for that by which shirk is forgiven, and the verse is for distinguishing between the two sins. Likewise, His statement: 'If you avoid the major sins that you are forbidden from' (Quran 4:31) does not admit [what they say] because of the expiation [mentioned] in it, and what has no sin does not expiate, and error does not actualize sin, and expiation is for something by which one is recompensed. And it does not admit what the Mu'tazila said, because their statement prevents the realization of the likeness; since they occur from one who avoids major sins as forgiven, and in this is their establishment, then expiation, while they make them forgiven [?], not expiated; for the forgiven is that which is covered over, and in its remaining for a period is its repulsion, and the expiated is that which comes from its doer a good deed by which it is expiated, like His statement: 'Those, Allah will replace their evil deeds with good deeds' (Quran 25:70), and His statement: 'Shall I guide you to a trade' (Quran 61:10), and His statement: 'If you give charity openly' (Quran 2:271), to the mention of expiation, and likewise His statement: 'Repent to Allah with sincere repentance' (Quran 66:8), 'Indeed good deeds remove evil deeds' (Quran 11:114). And after, the verse does not admit the statement of the Mu'tazila, because they make the persistent [?] the owner, and whoever does not persist in it is repenting from it, regretful of it, and in incitement that He forgives with repentance, and all sins are forgiven by it. And the two [verses] proceed with differentiation indicating: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' and His statement: 'If you avoid the major sins that you are forbidden from', so He, and Allah knows best, that shirk is not forgiven except through repentance from it, and other [?] is permissible or increased by other than it from good deeds so that the statement is correct with the realization of benefit to return [?] power except with Allah by favor [of] Quran, and no. Then for the verse are aspects that prevent the statement of the Mu'tazila and the Khawarij. One of them is that He said: 'If you avoid the major sins that you are forbidden from', and in that is no clarification of the ruling of one who does not avoid. It became clear that major sins are of two types: one is major sins in belief from the types of disbelief and denial by which the disbelievers differed, and another is major sins of actions whose doer avoids them by avoidance [?] in that he considers them as Allah has made them upon him of greatness of action and sin, and this is avoidance, and he has committed them by action, so it is commission, and in the verse there is no aspect of avoidance, so it is possible that one is praised: that he avoids major sins of belief, and they are types of shirk, and He expiates for them other than them for whomever He wills. That is from good deeds or by favor as we explained in one of the two verses with expiation and in the other with forgiveness. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 936

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij interpretations of two Quranic verses concerning forgiveness and major sins. The author begins with the verse 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' (4:48). He states that the Khawarij interpretation is corrupt because they misunderstand the nature of shirk—it is not a sin that can be forgiven by something other than repentance; the verse mentions forgiveness to distinguish shirk from other sins. Next, he turns to the verse 'If you avoid the major sins that you are forbidden from' (4:31). He argues that this verse does not support the Mu'tazila claim because it mentions expiation (takfir), and expiation applies to something that has a sin, but error (kata') does not constitute a sin. The Mu'tazila position that major sins are forgiven without expiation is rejected because it would imply that sins remain covered (maghfura) without being expiated (mukaffara), whereas the Quran shows that good deeds can expiate sins (e.g., 25:70, 61:10, 2:271, 66:8, 11:114). The author then clarifies that the two verses together show a distinction: shirk requires repentance, but other major sins may be forgiven through good deeds or divine favor (tafaddul). He then provides further analysis: the verse about avoiding major sins does not specify the ruling for one who does not avoid them, so it is inferred that major sins are of two types—those of belief (kufr) and those of actions. The avoidance of major sins in belief is praised, and such sins (shirk) can be expiated for others (i.e., minor sins) by good deeds or divine favor, as explained in the two verses. The passage concludes with a reminder of dependence on Allah.

شرك
توبة

Key Takeaway
Shirk requires repentance, but other major sins can be expiated through good deeds or Allah's favor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Maturidi says that avoiding a major sin includes recognizing its gravity even if you fall into it. Think of a sin you keep repeating. Do you truly see it as grave and hateful to Allah, or have you become numb to it? How can you cultivate that inner avoidance before you act again?

Al-Ka'bi on Iman and the Sunni Response
Maturidi presents al-Ka'bi's view on iman and begins a refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili view that iman is not derived from action and the early Sunni counter-statement that every act of obedience is from iman.

Core Idea
Iman is not merely an action; it is the root from which actions spring.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi engages with the Mu'tazili scholar al-Ka'bi, who argued that the name 'believer' is not derived from any action. Al-Maturidi begins to respond by affirming that every act of obedience is from iman, but the text is unfortunately cut off. Nevertheless, we can grasp the central point of contention: is iman a simple affirmation or does it inherently include deeds?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that the text has gaps; focus on the clear parts: al-Ka'bi's claim and the beginning of Maturidi's response.
Some brackets [١] indicate missing words, but the logical flow is discernible.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's definition of iman
Al-Ka'bi explicitly says 'our saying “believer” is not derived from the action.' He argues that it is an absolute name for anyone who affirms and obeys God. Find this in the text.
This is a key Mu'tazili position: iman is just affirmation, not including deeds.
3
Understand the Sunni response (ahl al-haqq)
Before al-Ka'bi's quote, Maturidi says 'the people of truth say: Every act of obedience is from iman.' This indicates that deeds are part of iman, not separate.
Maturidi's full response is cut off, but the opening clearly states the Sunni stance.
4
Reflect on the difference
Consider how these two views lead to different understandings of faith and practice. Does iman increase with good deeds? Does it decrease with sin?
This lesson only introduces the debate; later lessons may complete Maturidi's argument.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تقدم بيان ما له اسم الايمان . ثم نذكر بعض الذي ذكره الكعبي لمذهبه من القول الذي اختاره وارتضاه ، ثم الحجاج له بما يعلم من تأمله مبلغه في دين الله ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . أن قول أهل الحق : إن كل طاعة من الإيمان ، واسم الإيمان يستحق بما يوجب تركه فقط ، لما لا اسم يسمى الفسق قال : وليس قولنا : مؤمن، بمشتق من الفعل به كل من يصدق أحدا ، وأطاعه وخضع له اسما مطلقا ، أيضا [١]سمه فقط ؛ إذ لو كان [١]سمه الجاز التسمية به لمن ليس هو كما تسمى الحسناء قبيحة ، وإذا لم يكن كذلك ثبت أنه اسم مشتق من فعل ومدح فى الدين و[ا] سمه للتفرقة ولا هو كذلك قال الشيخ رحمه الله : نقول وبالله نستعين : قوله : أهل الحق كذا حق ، إن لم يرد بقوله : كل طاعة من الإيمان ، غير أنه من الإيمان ، وهو كما يقول :
And the explanation of what has the name of iman has preceded. Then we mention some of what al-Ka'bi mentioned for his school from the opinion he chose and approved, then the argument against him based on what one who reflects knows of his measure in the religion of God, and there is no power except with God. That the statement of the people of truth (ahl al-ḥaqq) is: 'Every act of obedience is from iman, and the name of iman is deserved only by what necessitates leaving it, because there is no name called fisq.' He (al-Ka'bi) said: 'And our saying: “believer” is not derived from the action; rather, everyone who affirms someone, obeys him, and submits to him is called by that name absolutely. Also [1] his name only; for if [1] his name, it would be permissible to name by it one who is not as when a beautiful woman is called ugly. And if it is not so, then it is established that it is a name derived from action and praise in the religion, and [1] his name for differentiation, and it is not so.' The Shaykh (Maturidi) – may God have mercy on him – said: 'We say, and with God we seek help: His statement: “the people of truth such and such truth” – if he does not mean by his statement “every act of obedience from iman” other than that it is from iman, and it is as he says:' [text ends]
Source pages p. 942

Commentary
This passage engages with the Mu'tazili scholar al-Ka'bi (Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi al-Balkhi). Maturidi first notes that the proper understanding of iman has already been explained. He then proposes to present some of al-Ka'bi's views and then refute them. The text includes a direct quotation from al-Ka'bi. Al-Ka'bi argues that the name 'believer' is not derived (mushtaqq) from any action; rather, it is an absolute name for anyone who affirms and obeys God. He then seems to argue that if it were derived, it could be applied incorrectly, but since it is not, it is a name derived from action and praise for differentiation. However, the text is garbled. Maturidi's response begins but is cut off in the OCR. He seems to clarify that the statement 'every act of obedience is from iman' is correct if it means that obedience is part of iman, not that iman is only obedience. This lesson is incomplete due to OCR issues.

Refutation on the condition of the wicked
Maturidi refutes an argument about the threat against the wicked.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the argument that the Murji'ah universally apply the threat to the wicked, and learn that they restrict it to those who deem sin lawful.

Core Idea
The threat is not for all sinners, only for those who deem sin lawful.

Maturidi engages with an opponent who claims that the Murji'ah agree the wicked who dies without repentance is subject to one of two threats. He refutes this by showing the opponent's narration about the Murji'ah is actually false; in fact, most Murji'ah deny that the threat applies to anyone except those who deem forbidden things lawful. This passage illustrates Maturidi's critical method and his attention to precise theological positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: first the opponent's argument, then the refutation.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فإذ كان القول عنهم هو خد ما حكيت'.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent says that by consensus the wicked person is subject to one of two threats, and that the Murji'ah consider it possible that the threat applies to him, except Muqatil who says he is inevitably from the people of promise.
This is presented in the first part of the passage.
3
Follow the refutation
Maturidi responds that even if the premise were true, the opponent's narration about the Murji'ah is the opposite of their actual position, so his claim is corrupted. Then he states that most of those affiliated with Irja' deny the threat except for those who deem things lawful.
This is the key move: showing the narration is false.
4
Understand the implication
The passage clarifies that the threat (wa'id) is specifically for those who deem sins lawful, not for every sinner. This refutes the opponent's attempt to attribute a universal threat view to the Murji'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال: إذ ثبت بمتفق القول على أحد الوعيد الفاسق إن مات قبل أن يتوب بين أهل الإرجاء، وأن يجوز أن يكون هو المعنى به، ويجوز أن لا يكون إلا ما قال مقاتل: إنه من أهل الوعد لا محالة ولا يترك لمثله الإجماع إنه ليس بمؤمن.. فيقال له: لو كان بالذي ذكرته يثبت الذي ادعيت، فإذ كان القول عنهم هو خد ما حكيت يفسد ما ادعيت، ثم أكثر المنتسبين إلى الإرجاء ينكر الوعيد أن يكون في غير المستحلين معروف ذلك بينهم، فهو يوضح ما ادعينا عليه من الكذب في الحكاية، والله أعلم.
Then he said: 'Since it is established by agreed statement that one of the two threats [applies to] the wicked if he dies before repenting among the people of al-Irjā', and that it is possible that he is meant by it, and it is possible that it is not except what Muqātil said: that he is from the people of the promise inevitably, and the consensus does not leave for the like of him that he is not a believer.' So it is said to him: 'If by what you mentioned what you claimed were established, then since the statement about them is the opposite of what you narrated, it corrupts what you claimed. Then most of those affiliated with al-Irjā' deny that the threat is for other than those who deem things lawful, that is known among them, so it clarifies what we claimed against him of falsehood in narration, and Allah knows best.'
Source pages p. 945

Commentary
This passage presents an opponent's argument about the condition of the wicked person who dies without repentance, and the author's refutation. The opponent claims that based on consensus, the wicked person is subject to one of the two threats. The author responds that even if that were true, the opponent's narration about the Murji'ah's position is false, and in fact most Murji'ah deny that the threat applies to those who do not deem forbidden things lawful. Thus the opponent's claim is based on false narration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Verse of Slander and the Definition of Kufr as Expulsion
Maturidi interprets the verse of slander and defines kufr as expulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's interpretation of the verse of slander and his nuanced definition of kufr as expulsion, distinguishing between sin and disbelief.

Core Idea
Kufr is not every sin; it is expulsion from mercy.

Maturidi engages with a Quranic verse on slander to clarify a critical theological principle: disbelief (kufr) is not defined by any sin or curse, but by the specific state of being expelled from divine mercy. In this passage, he interprets the verse in its historical context and then lays down the foundational definition of kufr, using the example of a person who utters a curse to illustrate his point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall flow of Maturidi's argument.
2
Identify the definition of kufr
Find the statement where Maturidi says 'al-asl anna al-kufr huwa al-tard'. This is the core principle.
This is the central doctrinal point.
3
Understand the qualifications
Notice that Maturidi immediately qualifies the definition: not every mardud (one who is expelled) by sin, and not everyone who says 'la'nat Allah' actually deserves it.
These qualifications prevent overgeneralization.
4
Examine the example
Consider the example of the person who said the curse—Maturidi argues that even if someone deserved the curse, the verse only mentions the saying, not the actual occurrence. This shows the difference between threat and reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكذلك الآخر. على أن الآية نزلت في المنافقين بقوله: فَأُولَئِكَ عِنْدَ اللَّهِ هُمُ الكَاذِبُونَ، ولا كل قاذف كذلك. وجملة ذلك أن من اجترى على ذلك القول واستخف بمقت الله ولعنه حلت به. والأصل أن الكفر هو الطرد، ولا كل مطرود بارتكاب مأثم، ولا يقبل لو عذب قدر ما استوجب، ولا كل من يقول عليه لعنة الله يستحقها، ولو كان أحد يستحقها كان أحقها بها صاحب هذا القول؛ إذ هو معلوم أنه كان يتعاطى الفسق ويختلف إلى الأئمة الحائرة، وكل ذلك على مذهبه يوجب اللعن حقيقة، وما فى الآية قول اللعن لا حقيقة الوقوع، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Likewise the other. That the verse was revealed concerning the hypocrites by His saying: 'Those are, in the sight of Allah, the liars', and not every slanderer is like that. The sum of that is that whoever dares to say that speech and treats lightly the wrath of Allah and His curse, it befalls him. The principle is that disbelief (al-kufr) is expulsion (al-tard), and not everyone expelled by committing a sin, and it is not accepted if He punishes him to the extent he deserved, and not everyone who says 'the curse of Allah be upon him' deserves it. And if anyone deserved it, the one most deserving of it would be the one who said this statement; for it is known that he used to engage in sin and frequent the misguided imams, and all of that according to his school necessitates the curse in reality. And what is in the verse is the saying of the curse, not the reality of its occurrence. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 947

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the interpretation of a Quranic verse regarding slander (qadhf) and the definition of kufr. Maturidi begins by stating that the verse '...those are the liars' (Quran 24:13) was revealed concerning hypocrites, not every slanderer. He then summarizes: whoever dares to say such a thing and shows contempt for Allah's wrath and curse, the curse befalls him. But the principle (asl) is that kufr is expulsion (tard), meaning being driven from mercy. However, not everyone who commits a sin is expelled; the punishment may fit the crime, but the person is not necessarily eternally damned. Also, not everyone who utters 'curse of Allah be upon him' actually deserves that curse. Maturidi gives an example: if anyone deserved the curse, it would be the one who made that statement (perhaps referring to an opponent), but even so, the verse only mentions the saying of the curse, not its actual occurrence. Thus, the threat is not automatically realized. The passage ends with the phrase 'la quwwata illa billah' (no power except with Allah).

Key Terms
كفر
طرد
لعن
مقت
قاذف
مأثم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any sin leading to divine curse expels the person from faith.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that kufr is specifically expulsion, and not every sin causes that; the curse mentioned in the verse is a saying, not a certain occurrence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the principle (asl) of kufr?
A) Slander (qadhf)
B) Expulsion (tard)
C) Disobedience (ma'siya)
D) Hypocrisy (nifaq)

Key Takeaway
Kufr is defined as expulsion from mercy, not merely as sin or divine curse, and the Quranic threat of curse does not automatically entail its realization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been quick to judge someone who committed a sin, mentally expelling them from the circle of Allah's mercy? How does Maturidi's definition of kufr as expulsion challenge that attitude in your heart?
?
When you read a Quranic verse that threatens a curse on certain wrongdoers, do you automatically assume it applies to everyone who commits that sin? What does this passage teach you about the danger of applying divine threats without considering context and intention?
?
Is there a person in your life—perhaps a fellow Muslim with a public sin—whom you have written off as beyond hope? How does the distinction between a uttered curse and its actual occurrence force you to hold back from pronouncing divine judgment?

Maturidi on Disobedience and Judgment by Other Than Allah
Maturidi refutes opponent's misuse of threat verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the threat of eternal punishment in verses about disobedience and judgment is linked to rejection of divine law, not mere omission or suspension.

Core Idea
Punishment threats apply to those who reject divine law, not just those who fail to apply it.

Maturidi engages with an opponent who uses verses like 'Whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger' to claim that any disobedience leads to eternal punishment. He refutes this by pointing to the context of suspending legal punishments and the principle that threat verses are tied to rejection (istihlal or radd), not simply failure to act. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overall sense of Maturidi's argument.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Notice how the opponent uses general disobedience verses to argue for eternal punishment for any sin, large or small.
The opponent cites 'Whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger'.
3
Understand Maturidi's counter on disobedience
Maturidi points out that the verse is about suspending legal punishments (تعطل الحدود), not about minor sins. If the opponent interprets it figuratively, the same applies to other verses.
4
Apply to the judgment verse
See how Maturidi applies the same logic to 'Whoever does not judge by what Allah revealed' – the opponent diverts it to deeming things lawful (استحلال), but Maturidi insists on consistency.
5
Reflect on neglect of prayer
Consider Maturidi's argument that neglect (إضاعة) is defined as rejection (رد), not delay, based on the verse about repentance and establishment of prayer.
Acceptance (قبول) is key, not mere action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج بقوله: 'وَمَنْ يَعْصِ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَهُ' في تعطل الحدود، وقد ذكر في مثله الخلود في النار من غير ذكر كبيرة ولا صغيرة. فإن كان ذا على التأويل، فمثله الأول. مع ما قال الله في مثله: 'وَمَنْ لَمْ يَحْكُمْ بِمَا أَنَزَلَ اللهُ فَأُولَئِكَ هُمُ الكَافِرُونَ'، فذلك أيضا في تعطيل الحدود، وهو يأبى القول به، ويصرف الآية إلى الاستحلال، فمثله الذي ذكر، ومثله في احتجاجه بقوله: 'أَضَاعُوا الصَّلاةَ'، مع ما قال الله تعالى: 'فَإِنْ تَابُوا وَأَقَامُوا الصَّلاةَ' من الأخوة والتحلية واجب بالقبول دون الفعل، فكذلك الإضاعة تكون بالرد دون التأخير، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he argued with His saying: 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger' concerning the suspension of legal punishments, and he mentioned in its like eternal punishment in the Fire without mentioning major or minor. So if this is by interpretation, then the first is like it. Along with what Allah said in its like: 'And whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed – then those are the disbelievers', so that also is concerning the suspension of punishments, and he refuses to accept that, and he diverts the verse to deeming it lawful (istihlal), so the like is what he mentioned. And likewise in his argument with His saying: 'They neglected prayer', along with what Allah Almighty said: 'But if they repent and establish prayer' – from brotherhood and al-tahliya is obligatory by acceptance (qabul) not by the action, so likewise neglect occurs by rejection (rad) not by delay. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 947

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument (احتج) that verses threatening eternal punishment apply to any disobedience. Maturidi explains that the verse 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger' (Quran 4:14, 33:36, 72:23) is about suspending legal punishments (تعطل الحدود), and the opponent used it to claim eternal punishment for any sin, large or small. Maturidi says if this verse is interpreted figuratively, then the first (the verse about slander) is likewise. He then addresses the verse 'And whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed – then those are the disbelievers' (Quran 5:44). The opponent refuses to accept that this applies to one who suspends punishments; instead he diverts it to deeming forbidden things lawful (استحلال). Maturidi says the same applies to the opponent's argument about the verse 'They neglected prayer' (Quran 19:59). He points to the verse 'But if they repent and establish prayer' (Quran 9:11) – brotherhood and something else (perhaps 'zakat' misread as 'tahliya') are obligatory by acceptance (qabul), not by the physical act. Thus, neglect (إضاعة) occurs by rejection (رد) not by mere delay. The point is that the threat of punishment is linked to rejection, not just omission.

Key Terms
تعطل الحدود
خلود في النار
استحلال
إضاعة الصلاة
قبول
فعل
رد
تأخير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any disobedience or failure to apply divine law leads to eternal punishment (kufr).
Correction: Maturidi argues that punishment is for those who reject divine law (istihlal or radd), not for those who merely suspend or delay.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how should the verse 'وَمَنْ لَمْ يَحْكُمْ بِمَا أَنزَلَ اللَّهُ' be interpreted?
A) It applies to anyone who fails to judge by divine law
B) It is about the suspension of punishments
C) It refers to deeming it lawful (istihlal)
D) It is only about hypocrites

Key Takeaway
Threats of punishment in the Quran are directed at those who reject divine law, not merely those who fail to act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to apply Allah's law in your life, do you excuse it as a mere delay or oversight, or do you recognise the danger of it becoming a rejection? Ask yourself: Is there any area of Islam you have begun to treat as optional?
?
The opponent argued that any disobedience leads to eternal punishment. Have you ever fallen into the trap of despair, thinking your sins are too great? Or have you fallen into complacency, thinking small sins don't matter? Which extreme does this lesson call you to avoid?
?
Maturidi says acceptance (qabul) is what brings brotherhood, not just the action. Are you merely performing religious acts while your heart rejects or resents the command? What is one command of Allah that you find hard to accept willingly, and what will you do about it today?

Three Interpretations of the Killing Verse and the Qisas Proof
Maturidi gives three interpretations of the killing verse and a proof from qisas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's three interpretations of the intentional killing verse and how he uses the qisas verse to argue that the killer remains a believer.

Core Idea
The qisas verse shows the killer is still a brother, not an eternal disbeliever.

Maturidi engages with a key prooftext used to claim that intentional killing removes one from faith. He offers three possible interpretations of the verse, then provides his own counterproof from the qisas verse, preserving brotherhood and hope of mercy. This passage is crucial for understanding the Maturidi position on grave sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let Maturidi's argument structure become clear.
Notice the three 'أوجه' and the transition to 'دليل ما بينا'.
2
Identify the three interpretations
Find the three possible meanings Maturidi gives for the verse on intentional killing. Note that he presents them without fully endorsing them.
The third interpretation refers to disbelievers.
3
Examine the qisas proof
Maturidi cites Quran 2:178 to show that after prescribing qisas, Allah still calls the killer a brother and offers mercy. This contradicts the idea of eternal punishment.
Key phrases: 'فأبقى له اسم الأخوة' and 'تخفيف من ربكم ورحمة'.
4
Reflect on the Mu'tazila view
Maturidi notes the Mu'tazila position, which requires qisas after repentance and removes afterlife punishment. Compare with his own view.
This shows the sectarian context of the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج بقوله: 'وَمَنْ يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِنًا مُتَعَمِّدًا'، وقوله: 'يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لا تَأْكُلُوا أَمْوَالَكُمْ بَيْنَكُمْ بِالبَاطِلِ'، وما ذكر في أكل مال اليتيم أحد وجوه. فأما القتل العمد فله أوجه ثلاثة: أحدها أن يكون فيمن تعمد القتل لدينه، وهذا الخطأ في القتل، والله أعلم. والثاني أن يكون ذلك جزاؤه، ولله التفضل عليه بالعفو والمقابلة بالحسنات، ولا قوة إلا بالله. والثالث أن تكون الآية في الكفرة، وفي القصة دليل ذلك. ثم دليل ما بينا قوله تعالى: 'يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ القِصَاصُ'، وإنما يكتب عليهم إذا قتلوا قتل العمد، وأبقى لهم بعد القتل الإيمان. ثم قال: 'فَمَنْ عُفِيَ لَهُ مِنْ أَخِيهِ شَيْءٌ'، فأبقى له اسم الأخوة، ثم قال: 'ذَلِكَ تَخْفِيفٌ مِنْ رَبِّكُمْ وَرَحْمَةٌ'، أطمعه في رحمته جل وعلا، وبعيد أن يكون له مع خلود في النار. ثم رأى المعتزلة في ذلك إلزام القصاص بعد التوبة، وإزالة عقوبة الآخرة.
And he argued with His saying: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally', and His saying: 'O you who have believed, do not consume your wealth among yourselves unjustly', and what was mentioned about consuming the property of an orphan is one of the aspects. As for intentional killing, it has three aspects: one – that it applies to one who intentionally kills for his religion, and this is a mistake in killing, and Allah knows best. The second – that this is his recompense, and Allah has the bounty to forgive him and requite him with good deeds, and there is no power except with Allah. The third – that the verse refers to disbelievers, and in the story there is evidence of that. Then the proof of what we have explained is the saying of Allah Almighty: 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is legal retribution', and it is only prescribed for them if they commit premeditated murder, and He left for them after killing the faith. Then He said: 'But whoever is pardoned by his brother something', so He left for him the name of brotherhood. Then He said: 'That is a relief from your Lord and a mercy', He gave him hope in His mercy, Exalted and Glorified, and it is far that he should have eternal punishment in the Fire. Then the Mu'tazila held in that the obligation of retaliation after repentance and the removal of the punishment of the Hereafter.
Source pages p. 948

Commentary
This passage is a response to an opponent's argument (احتج) using the verse on intentional killing of a believer (Quran 4:93) and the verse on consuming wealth unjustly (Quran 4:29) and the orphan's property. Maturidi first says that intentional killing has three possible interpretations (أوجه ثلاثة). First: the verse refers to one who kills for religious reasons (i.e., apostasy), and that is a mistake in killing? Actually, the phrase 'وهذا الخطأ في القتل' is ambiguous; perhaps it means if one kills mistakenly, it's not the same. Maturidi says 'والله أعلم'. Second: the punishment mentioned is the just recompense, but Allah may forgive the killer and even requite him with good deeds. Third: the verse was revealed about disbelievers, as the context (qissa) indicates. Then Maturidi provides his own proof (دليل ما بينا) from the verse on qisas (retaliation) in Quran 2:178. He explains: Qisas is prescribed when believers commit intentional murder, but after that, Allah still affirms faith for them. Then the verse says 'but whoever is pardoned by his brother something' – this preserves the name of brotherhood (meaning the victim's family pardons him). Then the verse calls it a 'relief and mercy' from Allah, giving hope of mercy, which would be incompatible with eternal punishment. Maturidi then notes that the Mu'tazila held that after repentance, the punishment is the requirement of qisas (in this world) and removal of the afterlife punishment – implying they think the killer is threatened with eternal punishment unless he repents, but Maturidi argues that the qisas verse shows the killer remains a brother and has hope of mercy.

أوجه ثلاثة
قصاص
أخوة
تخفيف
رحمة
خلود في النار
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intentional killing automatically leads to eternal hellfire and loss of faith.
Correction: Maturidi shows the killer remains a brother and can be forgiven; the verse is interpreted in multiple ways and the qisas verse preserves brotherhood and hope of mercy.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the qisas verse (Quran 2:178) prove about the intentional killer?
A) He is no longer a believer
B) He has to be executed immediately
C) He remains a believer and is called a brother
D) He is eternally punished in the Fire

Key Takeaway
The qisas verse preserves brotherhood and hope of mercy for the intentional killer, showing he is not eternally condemned.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own sins, do you truly believe that none of them are beyond Allah's mercy? Or is there a hidden part of you that thinks some of your deeds are unforgivable? How does Maturidi's argument that Allah preserves brotherhood and gives hope of mercy to a murderer challenge that hidden doubt? Commit today to recite Allah's merciful attributes and ask for forgiveness for that sin you think is too big.
?
Think of a Muslim who has committed a major sin that you know of. Have you mentally cut them off from the brotherhood of faith, or do you still consider them your brother or sister in Islam? Maturidi insists the killer remains a brother. Who is someone you need to restore brotherly feelings toward, even if you disapprove of their actions? Make a concrete step to reach out to them this week.
?
The qisas verse gives hope of mercy for the most heinous crime. Where in your life have you lost hope in Allah's mercy? Perhaps you struggle with a recurring sin and feel it's too late? How does this lesson teach you that even if you 'kill' your own soul with sin, Allah's mercy can still reach you? Write down one specific sin you feel hopeless about and then write a prayer asking Allah for the same mercy He promises to the murderer.

Three Aspects of the Verse on Intentional Killing
Imam Maturidi enumerates three interpretations of Quran 4:93 and supports his view with Quran 2:178, refuting the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three interpretations of the verse on intentional killing (Quran 4:93) and how Imam Maturidi uses them to argue that a believer who commits such a sin is not necessarily eternally punished.

Core Idea
Intentional killing does not automatically remove faith; the verse allows for pardon and has multiple valid interpretations.

Imam Maturidi addresses a challenging verse that seems to threaten believers with eternal punishment for intentional killing. He presents three possible understandings of the verse that prevent it from contradicting the principle that faith can be retained despite major sins. He then supports his view with another verse on retribution, which refers to the killer as a 'brother' and promises mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic along with its literal translation. Notice the structure: three aspects are listed, then a supporting argument from another verse, and finally a reference to the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the three aspects
Find the three interpretations Imam Maturidi gives for the verse on intentional killing. What does each aspect suggest about the fate of the killer?
3
Examine the proof from retribution
Look at how Imam Maturidi uses Quran 2:178 to reinforce his argument. Note that the killer is still called a 'brother' and given hope for mercy.
4
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The passage ends with a note on how the Mu'tazila twist the verse. Consider why their interpretation is problematic according to Imam Maturidi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج بقوله : وَمَنْ يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِنًا مُتَعَمِّدًا ، وقوله : يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لا تَأْكُلُوا أَمْوَالَكُم بَيْنَكُمْ بِالبَاطِلِ ، وما ذكر في أكل مال اليتيم فأما القتل العمد فله أوجه ثلاثة : أحدها أن يكون فيمن تعمد القتل لدينه ، وهذا الخطأ في القتل ، والله أعلم . والثانى أن يكون ذلك جزاؤه ، ولله التفضل عليه بالعفو والمقابلة بالحسنات، ولا قوة إلا بالله . والثالث أن تكون الآية في الكفرة ، وفى القصة دليل ذلك ثم دليل ما بينا قوله تعالى : يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمْ القِصَاصُ : وإنما يكتب عليهم إذا قتلوا قتل العمد ، وأبقى لهم بعد القتل الإيمان . ثم قال : فَمَنْ عُفى لَهُ مِنْ أَخِيه شي ، فأبقى له اسم الأخوة، ثم قال : ذَلِكَ تخفيف تَخْفِيفٌ منْ رَبِّكُمْ وَرَحْمَةٌ ، أطمعه فى رحمته جل وعلا ، وبعيد أن يكون له مع خلود في النار ثم رأى المعتزلة فى ذلك إلزام القصاص بعد التوبة ، وإزالة عقوبة الآخرة و صرف الآية
And he argued with His saying: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally' (Quran 4:93), and His saying: 'O you who have believed, do not consume your wealth among yourselves unjustly' (Quran 4:29), and what is mentioned about consuming the property of an orphan. As for intentional killing, it has three aspects: one of them is that it is about one who intentionally kills for his religion, and this is an error in killing, and Allah knows best. The second is that it is his recompense, and Allah has the prerogative to pardon him and exchange with good deeds, and there is no power except with Allah. The third is that the verse is about the disbelievers, and in the story there is evidence for that. Then the proof of what we have stated is His saying (Exalted): 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is legal retribution' (Quran 2:178). He prescribes it for them when they commit intentional killing, and He keeps for them after the killing the faith. Then He said: 'But whoever is pardoned by his brother anything', so He kept for him the name of brotherhood. Then He said: 'That is an alleviation from your Lord and a mercy', so He gave him hope in His mercy, and it is unlikely that he would have eternal punishment in the Fire. Then the Mu'tazila took possession of that (arguing) that retribution is obligatory after repentance and removal of the punishment of the Hereafter, and they diverted the verse.
Source pages p. 948

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument about the verse on intentional killing (Quran 4:93) and its implications for the status of a believer who commits a grave sin. The author first presents three possible interpretations (wujuh) of the verse that prevent it from necessarily implying eternal punishment: (1) It may refer to a person who kills for his religion, which is an error in interpretation. (2) It may describe the recompense (jaza') of such killing, but Allah may pardon and replace with good deeds. (3) The verse may be about disbelievers, not believers. The author then strengthens his position by citing the verse on retribution (Quran 2:178). He argues that this verse prescribes retribution for intentional killing but still refers to the killer as a 'believer' and a 'brother' after the act, and even gives hope for mercy, making eternal punishment unlikely. Finally, he notes that the Mu'tazila twist the verse by claiming that after repentance, retribution becomes obligatory and removes the punishment of the Hereafter, but this is a misapplication (takhsis) of the verse.

Key Terms
قتل العمد
إيمان
قصاص
معتزلة
أخوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse on intentional killing means that any believer who commits murder is eternally punished without any hope of pardon.
Correction: Imam Maturidi shows that the verse has three interpretations that avoid eternal punishment, and the retribution verse continues to call the killer a brother and offers mercy.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Maturidi, which of the following is NOT one of the three aspects (wujuh) of the verse on intentional killing?
A) The verse may refer to one who kills for his religion.
B) The verse may describe the recompense, but Allah may pardon.
C) The verse may be about the punishment of the killer in this world only.
D) The verse may be about disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi argues that the verse on intentional killing does not automatically consign a believer to eternal punishment; multiple interpretations and the retribution verse support the possibility of pardon and continued faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever committed a sin so grave that you convinced yourself Allah could no longer accept you as His believing servant? How does this lesson's teaching about the permanence of faith challenge that assumption—and what will you do the next time that thought comes?
?
The Mu'tazila demanded retribution and repentance before they would consider a sinner as forgiven. Is there someone in your life whose faith you have judged as insufficient because of their mistakes? Will you now extend to them the same mercy Allah extends to the intentional killer who remains a believer?
?
If you kill someone in your heart through hatred or malice, do you still believe you are a believer? This passage says that even the one who takes a life is called a believer and a brother. What does that say about the depth of Allah's mercy—and how does it change the way you view fellow Muslims who have fallen into major sin?

Dismissal of the Objection Regarding Women
Maturidi dismisses an objection based on a specific case about women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi dismisses the objection based on the case of women as imagination and why rulings of disbelief cannot be used for general inference.

Core Idea
Exceptional cases do not prove general rules about disbelief.

Maturidi addresses an objection that tries to use a specific ruling about women to argue for a middle ground. He dismisses it as imagination, asserting that rulings of disbelief are diverse and cannot be used as evidence. This lesson examines his concise refutation and its implications for theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the tone and brevity of Maturidi's dismissal.
The passage is short but dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent cites a specific case about a woman, claiming it is exceptional (makhsus). Understand what this objection is trying to do—using a particular ruling to argue for a middle position.
The 'woman' case is likely a reference to a legal exemption.
3
Understand Maturidi's dismissal
Maturidi calls it imagination (khayal) and states that rulings of disbelief (ahkam al-kufr) are diverse and cannot be used as evidence for a general principle. He also notes that the opponent's attempts to refute the Khawarij were unsuccessful.
This shows Maturidi's method of cutting off weak arguments quickly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بالمرأة فزعم أنها مخصوص، وهذا النوع من الخيال، بل أحكام الكفر مختلفة لا يستدل بها على شيء. وقد رأيته أطنب في معارضات الخوارج، وتكلف الخروج مما قابلوه به، مما يعلم اكل من تأمله أنه لم يحقق ما رام الرامي، ولا تخلص مما قوبل به حق التخلص فأغضيت عن ذكره.
Then he objected against himself by [the case of] the woman, claiming that it is specific, and this type is imagination. Rather, the rulings of disbelief are different; they are not used to infer anything. And I saw that he elaborated in refuting the Khawarij and went to great lengths in extricating himself from what they confronted him with, such that anyone who reflects knows that he did not realize what the opponent aimed for, nor did he extricate himself properly, so I refrained from mentioning it.
Source pages p. 951

Commentary
This passage is a brief refutation of a specific objection. Maturidi says that the opponent (presumably someone arguing for a middle ground) presented an objection concerning a woman (likely a ruling about women being exempt from certain punishments or similar), claiming that this case is exceptional (makhsus). Maturidi dismisses this as a type of imagination (khayal). He asserts that the rulings of disbelief (ahkam al-kufr) are diverse and cannot be used as evidence for a general principle. He then remarks that he has witnessed the opponent extensively refuting the Khawarij and struggling to extricate himself from their arguments, but he did not succeed in achieving the opponent's aim or escaping properly. Therefore, Maturidi declines to mention those futile attempts.

Key Terms
عارض
مخصوص
خيال
أحكام الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a specific exemption in rulings can be generalized to prove a middle ground between faith and disbelief.
Correction: Maturidi warns that exceptional cases (makhsus) are not valid evidence; rulings of disbelief are diverse and context-dependent, and cannot be used for universal inference.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi characterize the opponent's objection regarding the woman?
A) As a valid scholarly opinion
B) As imagination
C) As a clear proof
D) As a consensus

Key Takeaway
Exceptional cases cannot be used to infer general principles in matters of faith and disbelief; each ruling stands on its own.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you recall a moment when you used an exceptional case to justify a shortcut in your religious practice? How does Maturidi's warning against inferring general principles from specific rulings challenge that justification? What will you do next time you face that temptation?
?
Maturidi dismisses the opponent's argument as 'imagination'. Is there an area of your faith where you rely on imaginative reasoning—perhaps an excuse or a far-fetched interpretation—to avoid the clear truth? Ask Allah to replace that with sincere submission, and commit to acting on what you know today.
?
The opponent struggled to extricate himself from the Khawarij's arguments. Are you caught in debates or doubts about faith that you cannot resolve? Perhaps you have not grounded yourself in the established principles of Ahl al-Sunnah. What concrete step will you take this week to strengthen your foundation in sound 'aqeedah?

Sects and Disagreements
beginner
4 min
Maturidi's Critique of al-Ka'bī's Allegories
Maturidi introduces his refutation of al-Ka'bī's allegories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's characterization of al-Ka'bī's allegorical interpretations as deliberate attempts to deny faith to sinners and cause despair of God's mercy.

Core Idea
Al-Ka'bī's allegories are strategic tools to exclude sinners from faith and mercy.

Maturidi turns to refute a Mu'tazilite contemporary, al-Ka'bī, who employed forced allegorical readings of the Quran. According to Maturidi, these interpretations are not innocent exegesis but calculated moves to strip sinners of the name of faith and push them toward despair. This short passage sets the stage for a detailed refutation that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the key terms and the tone of condemnation.
Pay attention to the phrase 'far-fetched allegories' – it signals Maturidi's view of al-Ka'bī's method.
2
Identify al-Ka'bī's alleged motives
According to Maturidi, what does al-Ka'bī aim to achieve? List the two outcomes: removing the name of faith and causing despair of mercy.
3
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why this debate matters: who is considered a believer? What is the consequence of despairing of God's mercy?
This connects to broader debates about faith and major sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر ما ذكر الكعبي فيما احتج عليه من القرآن بالحيل المستبعدة؛ ليصرف عن نفسه واتباعه اسم الإيمان ويوجب الإياس عن رحمة الله والاختيار لعداوته بكبيرة كأنه به يحصل على نفع في الدنيا وحمد في الدين، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then we will mention what al-Ka‘bī mentioned in what he argued from the Quran by far-fetched allegories, in order to divert from himself and his followers the name of faith and to necessitate despair of Allah's mercy and the choice of enmity towards Him due to a major sin, as if he thereby obtains benefit in this world and praise in religion. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 954

Commentary
This short passage functions as a transition to a new section. Maturidi announces that he will now mention (and then refute) the arguments of al-Ka‘bī—a Mu‘tazilite theologian—who used far-fetched allegorical interpretations (ḥiyal mustabʿada) of the Quran to support his doctrine. According to Maturidi, al-Ka‘bī employs these forced interpretations strategically: his goal is to remove the name of faith (īmān) from himself and his followers by asserting that committing a major sin expels one from faith entirely, thus causing them to despair of Allah's mercy. Al-Ka‘bī also portrays the act of declaring enmity towards God (presumably the sinner becomes an enemy of God) as a deliberate choice. Maturidi implies that al-Ka‘bī seeks worldly benefit and religious praise through this rigid stance, but the truth is otherwise. The phrase 'lā ḥawla wa lā quwwata illā billāh' (there is no power except with Allah) is a standard formula expressing reliance on God. This passage sets up a detailed refutation that follows in subsequent pages.

Key Terms
الكعبي
الحيل المستبعدة
الإيمان
الإياس
العداوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ka'bī's allegorical interpretations are just a harmless method of exegesis.
Correction: Maturidi argues that al-Ka'bī's 'far-fetched allegories' are deliberately used to deny faith to sinners and cause despair of God's mercy, thus they have serious theological consequences.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does al-Ka‘bī aim to achieve by his far-fetched allegorical interpretations?
A) To prove that major sins do not affect faith
B) To divert from himself and his followers the name of faith and cause despair of Allah's mercy
C) To increase the rewards of believers in the Hereafter
D) To establish the justice of God

Key Takeaway
Maturidi exposes al-Ka'bī's allegorical method as a deliberate attempt to deny faith to sinners and push them toward despair of God's mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a complicated argument to justify excluding someone from God’s mercy? How does Maturidi’s exposure of al-Ka‘bī’s motives force you to examine your own intentions when interpreting religious texts? Will you now scrutinize your heart before engaging in theological debate?
?
Maturidi implies that al-Ka‘bī’s method is driven by a desire for worldly benefit and praise. Think of your own study of Islam: are there times when you seek recognition or status through theological discussions? What would it take to purify your intention solely for Allah?
?
Despair of Allah’s mercy is a dangerous spiritual state. Have you ever felt that your sin is too great for forgiveness? How does this lesson reveal that such despair can be a tactic of an enemy, not a truth from Allah? What step will you take today to reaffirm your hope in His mercy?
